وفي الإيضاح للكرماني من كتاب الصرف لو عقد السلم بلا أجل فهو فاسد فإن جعلا له أجلاً معلوماً قبل أن يتفرقا جاز إن كانت الدراهم قائمة بعينها لأن الدراهم فيه قائمة مقام المبيع فلا بد أن تكون بحيث يبتدأ فيها العقد، فهذه تسعة شرائط. والعاشر بيان قدر الأجل. والحادي عشر بيان مكان الإيفاء فيما له حمل ومؤنة وهو خاص بالمسلم فيه وسيأتي. والثاني عشر قبض رأس المال قبل الافتراق وسنذكره. والثالث عشر أن لا يشمل البدلين إحدى علتي الربا لأن انفراد أحدهما يحرم النساء والرابع عشر أن لا يكون فيه خيار شرط. وفي البزازية ويبطله شرط الخيار فإن أسقطه قبل الافتراق ورأس المال قائم في يد المسلم إليه صح وإن هالكاً لا ينقلب صحيحاً. الخامس عشر أن يتعين المسلم فيه بالتعيين فلا يصح السلم في النقدين وفي التبر روايتان. وذكر في المعراج وفتح القدير من شرائط رأس المال كون الدراهم منتقدة عند أبي حنيفة مع إعلام القدر اهـ. وليس المراد به تعجيل رأس المال لأن صاحب المعراج ذكر شرط التعجيل والقبض وحده وذكر الانتقاد شرطاً، وإنما المراد به معرفة الجيد من الرديء منه فلو لم ينقدها لم يصح. ويشكل عليه قولهم في تعليل قول الإمام أن الإشارة إلى رأس المال لا تكفي لاحتمال أن يجد البعض زيوفاً فيحتاج إلى الرد ولا يتيسر الاستبدال إلا بعد المجلس فإن هذا يقتضي عدم اشتراط الانتقاد أو لا فليتأمل. السادس عشر وجود المسلم فيه من حين العقد إلى حين المحل كما في المعراج وقد تقدم مفهومه بقوله والمنقطع». والسابع عشر أن يكون ما يضبط بالوصف وهو أن يكون من الأجناس الأربعة المكيل والموزون والمذروع والمعدود المتقارب وتقدم أول الباب، وقد ذكره في الشرائط في المعراج الثامن عشر بيان قدر رأس المال في المثليات عنده كما سيأتي.
And in al-Iydah of al-Kirmani from the book of Sarf: If they contract salam without a term, it is invalid. If they make a known term before they separate, it is permissible if the dirhams are still in existence, because the dirhams therein take the place of the commodity, so they must be such that the contract can begin with them. So these are nine conditions. The tenth is specification of the duration of the term. The eleventh is specification of the place of delivery for that which has carriage and cost, and it is specific to the commodity and will come. The twelfth is receipt of the capital before separation, and we will mention it. The thirteenth is that both counterparts should not include one of the causes of riba, because the isolation of one of them prohibits nasi'ah (delayed payment). The fourteenth is that there be no option of stipulation in it. In al-Bazzaziyya: it is invalidated by the stipulation of an option; if he drops it before separation while the capital remains in the hand of the seller, it becomes valid; but if it is destroyed, it does not become valid. The fifteenth is that the commodity be specifically identified, so salam is not valid in gold and silver coins (naqdain), and regarding bullion there are two narrations. Mentioned in al-Mi'raj and Fath al-Qadir from the conditions of the capital is that the dirhams be scrutinized (muntaqidah) according to Abu Hanifa, along with informing of the quantity. End. And the intent is not the promptness of the capital, because the author of al-Mi'raj mentioned the condition of promptness and receipt separately, and mentioned scrutiny as a condition. Rather, the intent is to know the good from the bad. So if he does not scrutinize them, it is not valid. This is problematic in light of their statement in explaining the Imam's saying that pointing to the capital is insufficient because it is possible that some forged coins are found, so they need to be returned and replacement is not possible except after the session. This implies non-requirement of scrutiny, or reflect. The sixteenth is the existence of the commodity from the time of contract to the time of delivery, as in al-Mi'raj, and its meaning has already been presented by his saying "and al-Munqati' (the interrupted)." The seventeenth is that it be definable by description, i.e., it must be from the four categories: measured by volume, weighed, measured by length, and countable nearly uniform; this was presented at the beginning of the chapter. He mentioned it in the conditions in al-Mi'raj. The eighteenth is specification of the quantity of the capital in fungible goods (mithliyyat) according to him, as will come.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage lists the many conditions required for a valid salam contract, expanding on the earlier five. It begins with a quote from al-Iydah discussing that adding a term after contracting but before separation can validate an invalid salam if the capital is still present. Then it enumerates conditions from the tenth to the eighteenth. These include: (10) specifying the term's duration, (11) specifying the place of delivery for heavy goods, (12) receipt of capital before the parties separate, (13) avoiding riba causes in both counterparts, (14) no stipulation of an option (khiyar al-shart), (15) the commodity must be specifically identified (so not in gold/silver coins), (16) commodity must exist from contract to delivery, (17) commodity must be from the four measurable categories, (18) for the capital if it is fungible, its quantity must be specified. Each condition is explained with references to earlier works. The passage emphasizes that salam is a restricted concession, requiring strict compliance to avoid uncertainty and riba.

Key Terms
خيار الشرط (khiyar al-shart): An option to cancel the contract within a stipulated period, which invalidates salam.
النقدين (al-naqdayn): Gold and silver coins (dinar and dirham); salam is not valid in them as commodity.
المثليات (al-mithliyyat): Goods that have equivalents in the market, like grains; quantities must be specified.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Hanafi fiqh, what is the ruling if a salam contract includes a stipulation of an option (khiyar al-shart)?
A) It is valid if the option is short-term
B) It invalidates the contract, but can become valid if the option is dropped before separation and capital is intact
C) It is valid only if both parties agree
D) It is valid because salam is a type of sale

Key Takeaway
A valid salam contract must satisfy numerous conditions beyond the basic five; ignorance of any condition may render the entire contract fasid.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Conditions of Salam: Term, Capital Amount, and Place of Delivery
Commentary on three conditions of salam sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three key conditions of salam sale (term, capital specification, place of delivery), apply the Hanafi minimum term rule, and distinguish the intra-madhhab dispute on capital specification.

Core Idea
Salam requires a term of at least one month; capital amount must be explicit according to Imam Abu Hanifa, though pointing suffices for the Sahibayn.

In this passage, the commentator from al-Bahr al-Raiq unpacks three essential conditions for a valid salam (forward sale) contract: the deferred term, the specification of the upfront capital, and the place of delivery. Each condition has been subject to debate even within the Hanafi school. Understanding these conditions is crucial because salam involves advanced payment for future delivery, and any ambiguity can invalidate the contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three conditions
Extract from the passage the three conditions discussed: a) the term (ajal) and its minimum, b) the specification of capital amount (qadr ra's al-mal), c) the place of delivery (makan al-ifa').
These are additional requirements beyond the general contract validity.
2
Analyze the proof for the minimum term
The text cites an oath scenario: if one swears to pay immediately and pays before the month ends, the oath is fulfilled. This shows 'immediate' is less than a month, so a genuine salam term must be at least a month.
The dalil is an example (mithal) from custom, not a direct text.
3
Examine the dispute on capital specification
Imam Abu Hanifa requires explicit statement of the capital amount; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say pointing suffices. The text gives Imam's reasoning: if part capital is returned due to defect, the remainder becomes ambiguous.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
4
Understand the place of delivery debate
The commentator discusses whether the place condition benefits the buyer (to avoid transport costs) or the seller. The preferred view is that it is the buyer's right, except in necessity.
The place condition is not a pillar but a stipulation (shart) for the buyer's benefit.
5
Apply the ruling
Given a scenario, determine validity: e.g., salam with a 20-day term is invalid; salam where capital is pointed to without exact amount is fasid according to Imam but valid for Sahibayn.
The mufta bihi for term is one month; for capital, Imam's view is precautionary but Sahibayn's may be relied upon in need.

أيام وقيل ما تراضيا عليه، وقيل أكثر من يوم، وقيل المرجع العرف وما في الكتاب هو الأصح وبه يفتى. وفي البناية وقال الصدر الشهيد في طريقته المطولة : والصحيح ما رواه الكرخي أنه مقدار ما يمكن فيه تحصيل المسلم فيه اهـ. فقد اختلف التصحيح لكن المعتمد ما في الكتاب وفي فتح القدير بعد نقل تصحيح الشهيد وهو جدير أن لا يصح لأنه لا ضابط محقق فيه وكذا ما عن الكرخي من رواية أخرى أنه ينظر إلى مقدار المسلم فيه المقصود من الأجل وهو القدرة على تحصيله. وفي القنية : لقي رب السلم المسلم إليه بعد حلول الأجل في غير البلد الذي شرط الإيفاء فيه فله مطالبته بالمسلم فيه إن كان قيمته في ذلك المكان مثل قيمته في المكان المشروط أو دونه لأن شرط المكان حق رب السلم دفعاً لمؤنة الحمل. قال رضي الله تعالى عنه : وأفتى بعض مفتي زماننا أنه لا يتمكن من مطالبته لأن تعيين المكان حق المسلم إليه دفعاً لمؤنة الحمل. وهذا الجواب أحب إلي إلا في موضع الضرورة وهو أن يقيم المسلم إليه في بلد آخر فيعجز رب السلم عن استيفاء حقه ثم قال : هدانا الله إلى الرواية المنصوصة قوله : (وقدر رأس المال في المكيل والموزون والمعدود) أي وشرطه بيان قدر رأس المال إذا كان العقد يتعلق على مقداره عند الإمام. وقالا : تكفي الإشارة إليه كالثمن والأجرة والمذروع لأن الجهالة مع الإشارة لا تفضي إلى المنازعة وله أنها قد تفضي إليها بأن ينفق بعضه ثم يجد بالباقي عيباً فيرده ولا يتفق له الاستبدال في مجلس الرد فينفسخ العقد في المردود ويبقى في غيره ولا يدري قدره ليبقى العقد بحسابه فيفضي إلى جهالة المسلم فيه : نصف قوله : (أقول هو جدير بأن يصحح الخ) قال في منح :
His statement: (And its minimum is a month) meaning the minimum of the term is a month; that was narrated from Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, because what is less than that is immediate, and the month and above is a term, evidenced by the issue of the oath: if one swore to pay his debt immediately and he paid it before the completion of the month, he is truthful in his oath. Some say its minimum is three days, some say whatever they agree upon, some say more than a day, some say the reference is custom, and what is in the book is the most correct and according to it fatwa is given. In al-Binaya, al-Sadr al-Shahid said in his extended methodology: the correct is what al-Karkhi narrated that it is an amount in which the subject matter can be obtained. So verification differed, but the relied-upon is what is in the book. In Fath al-Qadeer after quoting the verification of al-Shahid: it is not worthy to be correct because there is no precise measure in it, and likewise from al-Karkhi another narration that one looks at the amount of the subject matter and the custom of people in deferring its like; all that opens the door to disputes unlike the specific amount of time. I say: it is worthy to be correct and relied upon, especially for things that cannot be obtained in a month, because then the estimation by it leads to failure to achieve the purpose of the term, which is the ability to obtain it. In al-Qunyah: the buyer of salam met the seller after the term had lapsed in a place other than the place stipulated for delivery; he has the right to demand the subject matter if its value in that place is like its value in the stipulated place or less, because the stipulation of place is the right of the buyer to avoid transport cost. Radhi Allahu Anhu said: A mufti of our time gave fatwa that he cannot demand it because specifying the place is the right of the seller to avoid transport cost. This answer is dearer to me except in a case of necessity, which is that the seller resides in another country and the buyer is unable to collect his right. Then he said: May Allah guide us to the transmitted narration. His statement: (And the amount of capital in measured, weighed, and counted [items]) i.e., its condition is to specify the amount of capital when the contract depends on its amount according to Imam. And they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: pointing to it suffices like the price and hire and measured [items], because ignorance with pointing does not lead to dispute; and in his (Imam's) view, it may lead to dispute because he spends some of it then finds a defect in the remaining and returns it, and he cannot find a replacement in the session of return, so the contract is rescinded regarding the returned part and remains regarding the rest, and he does not know its amount for the contract to continue accordingly, leading to ignorance of the subject matter. Half: His statement: (I say it is worthy to be correct) etc. He said in Manh al-Ghaffar: Our teacher's statement here is worthy of non-acceptance, because it only applies if the one who estimates by the month obliges estimation by it and prevents estimation by increase, but it is not so; because if it is not obtained within the month and they agree on an increase, it is permissible, and there is no impediment at all, so there is no place for his statement 'then the estimation by it leads to failure to achieve the purpose of the term' etc., as is not hidden. And he refuted it in al-Nahr also, where he said: it is refuted because the month is its minimum, not its maximum, to complete his claim. Al-Ramli said after quoting the first:
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq is a commentary on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding three conditions of salam sale: the term (ajal), the specification of capital amount (qadr ra's al-mal), and the place of delivery (makan al-ifa'). The commentator first addresses the minimum term. He states that the relied-upon position, attributed to Imam Muhammad, is that the minimum term is one month. Evidence comes from a parallel in oaths: if someone swears to pay a debt 'immediately', paying before the month ends fulfills the oath, proving that 'immediate' (in the custom) is less than a month. Other views exist (three days, agreement, custom), but the correct view is one month, and fatwa is given accordingly. The commentator then notes a difference of opinion on the correct minimum. Some scholars, like al-Sadr al-Shahid, adopt al-Karkhi's view that the term should be sufficient to procure the goods. However, the relied-upon position remains one month as in the matn. The commentator gives his own opinion that the one-month minimum is practical and can be extended by mutual agreement. Next, the passage discusses the place of delivery. If the buyer and seller are in different places after the term, the buyer can demand delivery in that place if the value is equal or less, because the place condition is for the buyer's benefit to avoid transport costs. Some muftis differ, saying the place is for the seller's benefit. The commentator prefers the latter except in necessity. Finally, the passage covers the specification of capital amount in measurables, weighables, and countables. Imam Abu Hanifa requires explicit specification of the amount, while the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say pointing suffices, as in other contracts. Imam's reasoning is that ignorance from pointing can lead to disputes, e.g., if part of the capital is returned due to defect and the contract continues on the remainder, the exact portion becomes unknown. The Sahibayn argue that pointing usually suffices because the ignorance is not likely to cause conflict. The commentator then includes a marginal note rebutting his earlier statement about the month being insufficient for some items. He clarifies that the month is a minimum, not a maximum, so if goods need more time, parties may agree to a longer term.

Key Terms
أجل (ajal): The term or deferred period in a salam contract.
رأس المال (ra's al-mal): The capital or price paid upfront in a salam contract.
مكان الإيفاء (makan al-ifa'): The place where the subject matter is to be delivered.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, what is the minimum term for a valid salam contract?
A) Three days
B) One week
C) One month
D) No minimum

Key Takeaway
Salam requires a minimum term of one month and clear specification of the capital amount; the place of delivery, if stipulated, is generally for the buyer's benefit.

Minimum Term in Salam Sale and Conditions of Capital Specification
Establishing the minimum term in salam with proof and ikhtilaf, plus a separate condition on capital specification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the minimum term in salam (one month) and explain its proof from the oath analogy, and identify the condition for specifying the capital amount and the dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
The minimum term in salam is one month, derived from the oath analogy; capital must be precisely specified according to Abu Hanifa, while the Sahibayn accept pointing.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq and its commentary tackles two key conditions in a salam contract: the minimum deferred term and the specification of the capital. The Hanafi school requires at least a month for the term, proven by the rule that less than a month is considered immediate in oaths. It then addresses the requirement to state the exact measure of the capital, where Abu Hanifa differs from his two disciples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two separate rulings
The passage first discusses the minimum term in salam, then transitions to the condition of specifying the capital amount. Note where one discussion ends and the other begins.
The transition occurs at 'قوله : (وقدر رأس المال ...)' which starts a new matn point.
2
Analyze the proof for the minimum term
The text provides a proof from the issue of oaths: if someone swears to pay a debt immediately and pays before a month, the oath is fulfilled. This shows that less than a month is considered immediate, so a valid deferred term in salam must be at least a month.
The proof is a qiyas (analogy) from the domain of oaths to salam contracts.
3
Examine the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the term
The text mentions alternative opinions: three days, whatever agreed, more than a day, or reference to custom. It then discusses a flexible approach by al-Sadr al-Shahid and al-Karkhi, which is rejected in Fath al-Qadir for lack of a precise standard.
The relied-upon view is one month, and fatwa is given accordingly.
4
Understand the capital specification condition
Imam Abu Hanifa requires precise specification (e.g., exact weight or measure) of the capital when the contract is based on measurement. The Sahibayn accept mere pointing (ishara) as sufficient, analogous to price and rent.
Abu Hanifa's reasoning: pointing may lead to ignorance if part of the capital is consumed and the remainder is defective.
5
Distinguish the practical consequences
The difference in views affects the validity of the salam contract. Under Abu Hanifa's view, a capital that is merely pointed to without specification renders the contract fasid (defective). Under the Sahibayn, it would be sahih.
The text does not explicitly give a tarjih, but the reliance on Imam's view suggests it is preferred for caution.

المشروط أو دونه لأن شرط المكان حق رب السلم دفعاً لمؤنة الحمل. قال رضي الله تعالى عنه : وأفتى بعض مفتي زماننا أنه لا يتمكن من مطالبته لأن تعيين المكان حق المسلم إليه دفعاً لمؤنة الحمل. وهذا الجواب أحب إلي إلا في موضع الضرورة وهو أن يقيم المسلم إليه في بلد آخر فيعجز رب السلم عن استيفاء حقه ثم قال : هدانا الله إلى الرواية المنصوصة قوله : (وقدر رأس المال في المكيل والموزون والمعدود أي وشرطه بيان قدر رأس المال إذا كان العقد يتعلق على مقداره عند الإمام. وقالا : تكفي الإشارة إليه كالثمن والأجرة والمذروع لأن الجهالة مع الإشارة لا تفضي إلى المنازعة وله أنها قد تفضي إليها بأن ينفق بعضه ثم يجد بالباقي عيباً فيرده ولا يتفق له الاستبدال في مجلس الرد فينفسخ العقد في المردود ويبقى في غيره ولا يدري قدره ليبقى العقد بحسابه فيفضي إلى جهالة المسلم فيه : قوله : (أقول هو جدير بأن يصحح الخ) قال في منح الغفار كلام شيخنا هنا جدير بعدم القبول لأنه إنما يتجه لو كان الذي يقدره بالشهر يوجب التقدير به ويمنع التقدير بالزيادة وليس كذلك لأنه إذا لم يحصل في مدة الشهر واتفقا على زيادة عليه ،جاز ولا مانع من ذلك أصلاً فلا موقع لقوله «فيؤدي التقدير به إلى عدم حصول المقصود من الأجل الخ كما لا يخفى اهـ. ورده في النهر أيضاً حيث قال : مدفوع بأن الشهر أدناه لا أنه أقصاه ليتم ما ادعاه .اهـ. قال الرملي بعد نقله الأول :
His saying: (And its minimum is a month) i.e., the minimum term is a month. That was narrated from Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) because what is less than that is immediate (ajil), and a month and above is a term (ajal), evidenced by the issue of an oath: if one swore to pay a debt immediately, and he paid it before the completion of a month, he would be fulfilling his oath. And it was said its minimum is three days, and it was said whatever they mutually agree upon, and it was said more than a day, and it was said the reference is to custom, and what is in the book (al-Kanz) is the most correct and is followed for fatwa. And in al-Binayah: al-Sadr al-Shahid said in his lengthy method: The correct view is what al-Karkhi narrated that it is the amount in which the commodity can be obtained. So correctness differs, but the relied upon is what is in the book. And in Fath al-Qadir after quoting the Shahid's correction, he said: It is not worthy of being correct because there is no precise control in it, and similarly what al-Karkhi narrated from another narration that one looks at the amount of the commodity and the people's custom in setting a term for the like, all that opens disputes, contrary to the specified measure of time. I say: It is worthy of being corrected and relied upon, except for things that cannot be obtained in a month, then the estimation by it leads to the loss of the purpose of the term, which is the ability to obtain it. And in al-Qunyah: The owner of the salam met the seller after the term expired in a place other than the one where delivery was stipulated, so he can demand the commodity if its value in that place equals or is less than its value in the stipulated place, because the stipulation of the place is the right of the salam owner to avoid the cost of carriage. He (the author) said: Some muftis of our time gave fatwa that he cannot demand it because specifying the place is the right of the seller to avoid the cost of carriage. And this answer is dearer to me except in a case of necessity, that the seller resides in another country and the salam owner is unable to collect his right. Then he said: May Allah guide us to the narrated text. His saying: (And [it is a condition] to specify the amount of the capital in items measured by volume, weight, and count) i.e., a condition is to clarify the amount of the capital when the contract is based on its measure according to Imam (Abu Hanifa). And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: Pointing to it is sufficient, like the price, rent, and measured items, because ignorance with pointing does not lead to dispute. And for him (Abu Hanifa) it may lead to it, as he might spend some of it, then find a defect in the remainder and return it, but he cannot exchange it in the session of return, so the contract is rescinded regarding the returned part, and it remains for the rest, and he does not know its measure to continue the contract proportionally, leading to ignorance of the commodity. His saying: (I say it is worthy of being corrected, etc.) said in Manh al-Ghaffar: our shaykh's speech here is worthy of being rejected because it would apply only if the one who estimates by a month makes the estimation by it and prevents estimation by increase, but that is not so, because if it is not obtained in a month and they agree to an increase, it is permissible, and there is no prohibition at all, so there is no place for his saying 'it leads to loss of the purpose of the term' etc. as is clear. And he refuted it in al-Nahr as well where he said: It is refuted because the month is its minimum, not its maximum, to make his claim complete. End. Al-Ramli said after quoting the first:
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage discusses the minimum term (ajal) required in a salam (forward sale) contract. The matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq states that the minimum is one month. The commentator explains that this ruling is attributed to Imam Muhammad, and the rationale is that anything less is considered immediate (ajil), while a month and above qualifies as a term (ajal). The proof is from the issue of oaths: if someone swears to pay a debt 'immediately' and pays before a month, his oath is fulfilled, indicating that less than a month is considered immediate. Several other opinions are noted: three days, whatever the parties agree upon, more than one day, or reference to custom. The preferred view according to the text is one month, and fatwa is given accordingly. The author then mentions a correction by al-Sadr al-Shahid that the correct term is the amount of time sufficient to obtain the commodity, but this is rejected in Fath al-Qadir because it lacks a precise standard and leads to disputes. The author defends the one-month ruling, arguing that for items obtainable within a month, the term is sufficient, but for those requiring more, the parties can agree to extend. A later objection from Manh al-Ghaffar holds that the author's reasoning would only work if the month were the maximum, not the minimum, which is not the case. The discussion ends with a quote from al-Ramli. The passage also briefly touches on a separate issue regarding the place of delivery when the seller is in a different city, citing two fatwas: one allowing the salam owner to demand delivery if the value is equal or less, the other requiring the seller's consent unless the seller resides elsewhere. The author prefers the latter view except in cases of necessity. This is a complex, multi-layered commentary typical of later Hanafi fiqh works.

Key Terms
أجل (ajal): The deferred time period for delivery in a salam contract
السلم (al-salam): A forward sale where the price is paid in advance for a commodity to be delivered later
عاجل (ajil): Immediate delivery, opposite of deferred (ajal)
الإشارة (al-ishara): Pointing, i.e., indicating the capital by reference rather than exact specification

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the proof for the one-month minimum term in salam according to the passage?
A) A Quranic verse prohibiting immediate delivery.
B) A hadith specifying one month for salam.
C) An analogy from oaths: paying before a month is considered immediate.
D) Consensus of the Sahibayn.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires a minimum term of one month in salam, based on the maxim that less than a month is immediate; alternative flexible approaches are rejected for lack of precision.

Must the Capital in Salam Be Quantified?
Clarifying the requirement to specify the quantity of capital in salam with Imam Abu Hanifa's view vs Sahibayn

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Imam Abu Hanifa's position on capital specification in salam, explain how it differs from the Sahibayn's view, and apply the distinction to concrete scenarios.

Core Idea
In salam, the capital must be quantified to avoid dispute upon potential rescission – mere pointing is insufficient.

This passage explores a key disagreement within the Hanafi school: whether the capital (ra's al-mal) in a salam contract must be explicitly quantified or whether pointing to it suffices. Imam Abu Hanifa requires specification to prevent future disputes, while the Sahibayn analogize to price and rent where pointing is acceptable. The text provides a deeper rationale and applies the principle to several branches.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Imam Abu Hanifa requires that the capital in a salam contract be known and specified by quantity when it involves measurable items (makil, mawzun, or ma'dud).
2
Identify the opposing view
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that pointing to the capital is sufficient, just as in the case of price (thaman) and rent (ujra).
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Their proof is qiyas: in price and rent, ignorance about the exact amount does not cause dispute when the item is pointed to, so the same applies to salam capital.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response distinguishes salam: the seller may fail to deliver, requiring partial or full rescission. Without knowing the exact capital, the amount to be returned becomes uncertain, leading to dispute.
Furthermore, the Hanafi school gives precedence to the Companion Ibn Umar's practice over analogy.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: if the capital is pointed to but not quantified, Sahibayn deem the contract valid; Imam Abu Hanifa deems it invalid. The relied-upon position is Imam's.
An exception is when the capital is a cloth – there the contract is not based on measure; if short, the seller has a choice, not a reduction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأولى أن يعلل للإمام بأنه ربما لا يقدر على تحصيل المسلم فيه فيحتاج إلى رد رأس المال فيجب أن يكون معلوماً. وأما ما ذكروه فمندفع بما قدمناه من أن الانتقاد شرط عنده، وقد قال بقول ابن عمر رضي الله عنهما وقول الفقيه من الصحابة مقدم على القياس بخلاف ما إذا كان رأس المال ثوباً لأن الذرع وصف فيه والمبيع لا يقابل الأوصاف فلا يتعلق العقد بقدره، ولذا لو عدد الذرعان فوجده المسلم إليه أنقص لا ينتقص من المسلم فيه شيء وإنما يخير المسلم إليه. ومن فروع المسألة إذا أسلم في جنسين ولم يبين رأس مال أحدهما بأن أسلم مائة في كر حنطة وشعير ولم يبين حصة واحد منهما من رأس المال لم يصح فيهما لأنه ينقسم عليهما باعتبار القيمة وهي تعرف بالحزر أو أسلم جنسين ولم يبين قدر أحدهما بأن أسلم دراهم ودنانير في مقدار معلوم من البر فبين قدر أحدهما ولم يبين الآخر لم يصح السلم فيهما لبطلان العقد في حصته ما لم يعلم قدره فيبطل في الآخر أيضاً لاتحاد الصفقة أو لجهالة حصة الآخر من المسلم فيه فيكون المسلم فيه مجهولاً، والمراد بالمعدود هنا ما لا يتفاوت آحاده لتعلق العقد بمقداره .
And it is more appropriate to give as a reason for the Imam (Abu Hanifa) that perhaps he (the seller) is unable to obtain the commodity, so he needs to return the capital, so it must be known. And as for what they mentioned, it is rebutted by what we have presented that inspection (al-intiqad) is a condition according to him, and he followed the saying of Ibn Umar (may Allah be pleased with them both), and the saying of a jurist among the Companions is given precedence over analogy. Contrary to if the capital is a cloth, because measurement is a description in it, and the sold item does not correspond to descriptions, so the contract is not based on its measure. Hence, if he counts the yards and the seller finds it less, nothing is deducted from the commodity, but the seller has a choice. Among the branches of the issue: if one makes salam in two types and does not specify the capital for each, e.g., he pays one hundred for a load of wheat and barley and does not specify the share of each from the capital, it is not valid for both because it is divided between them by value, which is known by estimation. Or if he pays two types (dinars and dirhams) for a known amount of wheat and specifies the amount of one but not the other, the salam is not valid for both because the contract is void in its share until its amount is known, so it is void in the other as well due to the unity of the transaction or due to ignorance of the other's share in the commodity, so the commodity is unknown. And what is meant by 'counted' here is that whose units do not vary, because the contract is based on its quantity.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage presents and defends the position of Imam Abu Hanifa regarding the specification of the capital (ra's al-mal) in a salam contract. The Imam requires that the amount of capital be explicitly stated when the contract involves items measured by volume (makil), weight (mawzun), or count (ma'dud). This is because the contract is intrinsically linked to the quantity of capital; if the capital is not known, the contract may lead to dispute. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue that merely pointing to the capital (al-ishara) is sufficient, just as in the case of price (thaman) or rent (ujra), because ignorance along with pointing does not typically cause conflict. The commentator offers a preferable reasoning for the Imam: the seller might fail to deliver the commodity and thus must return the capital; therefore, the capital must be known. This is supported by the practice of the Companion Ibn Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) and the principle that a Companion's juristic opinion takes precedence over analogy. The text then distinguishes the case of a cloth as capital: since length is a description, not the essence, the contract is not based on its exact measure. If the cloth is found shorter, the seller has a choice (khiyar) to rescind or accept, but the commodity price does not reduce. Several branches of the issue are given: if the capital is specified for two types but not allocated between them, the salam is invalid for both; if the capital consists of two currencies and only one is quantified, the contract is void due to ignorance. The passage concludes by defining 'ma'dud' (counted) as items whose individual units do not differ, because the contract depends on quantity. This is a typical Hanafi deep analysis of the conditions of validity in salam.

Key Terms
رأس المال (ra's al-mal): The capital or price paid in advance by the buyer in a salam contract
مكيل (makil): Items measured by volume (e.g., grains)
موزون (mawzun): Items measured by weight (e.g., metals, produce)
معدود (ma'dud): Items counted individually, but whose units do not vary; thus requiring specification of quantity

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, why must the capital in a salam contract be specified when it is a measurable item?
A) Because pointing is forbidden
B) Because if the seller cannot deliver, the capital must be returned and must be known
C) Because the Qur'an requires it
D) Because the Sahibayn said so

Key Takeaway
In salam contracts, the capital must be known and specified to avoid invalidity due to ignorance, per the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Place of Delivery in Salam for Heavy Goods
Clarifying the ruling on specifying the place of delivery when goods require carriage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on specifying the place of delivery for heavy goods in salam contracts and explain the reasoning of Imam Abu Hanifa vs. the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
For heavy salam goods, the place of delivery must be specified; otherwise, the contract is invalid according to Imam Abu Hanifa.

In salam contracts, the place of delivery becomes a point of contention when the goods are heavy. Imam Abu Hanifa required its specification to avoid disputes over carriage costs, while his two students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, considered the contract location sufficient. This lesson unpacks their proofs and the practical difference in rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition in dispute
Determine what condition Imam Abu Hanifa requires and the Sahibayn reject regarding salam for heavy goods.
The condition is specifying the place of delivery (makan al-ifa').
2
Analyze Imam Abu Hanifa's evidence
Understand why Imam Abu Hanifa deems specification necessary: to avoid ambiguity (gharar) over transportation costs.
Heavy goods entail significant carriage expense; without specification, dispute may arise.
3
Analyze the Sahibayn's evidence
Examine their analogy to other liabilities (loans, sales of specific items, usurpation) that are fulfilled at the contract location.
They argue the place of obligation is the place of performance.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical consequence: if place is not specified, according to Imam the contract is fasid; according to Sahibayn, it is valid and delivery is at the contract location.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Establish which view is relied upon in the Hanafi school. The matn follows Imam Abu Hanifa, so his position is mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ومكان الإيفاء فيما له حمل من الأشياء أي وشرطه بيان مكان الإيفاء في المسلم إليه إذا كان له حمل ومؤنة أي إذا كان نقله يحتاج إلى أجرة والحمل بالفتح الثقل . قال في البناية : يعنون به ما له ثقل يحتاج في حمله إلى ظهر وأجرة حمال والمؤنة الكلفة وقالا : لا يحتاج إلى تعيينه ويسلمه في موضع العقد لأن مكانه مكان الالتزام فيتعين لإيفاء ما التزمه في ذمته كموضع الاستقراض والاستهلاك، وكبيع الحنطة بعينها وكالغصب والقرض وله أن
His saying: 'And the place of delivery for what has weight of items' i.e., a condition is to clarify the place of delivery of the commodity if it has weight and cost, i.e., if its transportation requires a fee. And 'haml' with the fatha is heaviness. He said in al-Binayah: They mean by it that which has weight requiring a back and the fee of a porter, and 'mu'na' is the cost. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: It does not need to be specified, and he delivers it at the place of the contract because that place is the place of obligation, so it is determined for fulfilling what he is obliged in his dhimmah, like the place of borrowing and consumption, and like the sale of specific wheat, and like usurpation and loan, and he has the right to...
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage discusses the condition of specifying the place of delivery (makan al-ifa') in a salam contract when the subject matter is heavy (dhu haml) and requires carriage. According to Imam Abu Hanifa (implied by the text 'wa sharthuhu'), it is a condition (shart) to specify the place of delivery in such cases. This is because the cost of transportation (ujrat) is a significant factor; without specifying the place, there is ambiguity that could lead to dispute. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) disagree: they hold that specification is not required, and the seller can deliver at the location of the contract. Their reasoning is that the place of contract is the place where the obligation was incurred, so it is naturally the place for fulfilling the debt-like obligation (ilzam fi al-dhimmah). They draw analogy to other situations: a loan (qard) or borrowing (istiqrad) – the borrower repays at the place of the loan; consumption (istihlak) – the compensation is due at the place of consumption; selling specified wheat – delivery at the place of contract; usurpation (ghasb) – return of the usurped item at the place of usurpation; and loan (qard) – repayment at the place of contract. The matn states that for heavy goods, place must be specified. The commentary from al-Binayah defines 'haml' as heavy items requiring labor and cost. This is a clear inter-madhhab dispute within the Hanafi school between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two students. The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is that of Imam Abu Hanifa, as stated in the matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq, which is a foundational text.

Key Terms
مكان الإيفاء (makan al-ifa'): The place where the seller is obligated to deliver the salam goods
حمل (haml): Heaviness or weight that necessitates carriage costs
مؤنة (mu'na): Cost or expense incurred for transportation

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) For all goods
B) For goods that are heavy and require carriage
C) Only for perishable goods
D) For goods that can be carried by hand

Key Takeaway
The specification of the delivery place is a condition for validity in salam when the goods are heavy, per the relied-upon Hanafi view.

Conditions for Valid Salam and Rulings on Two Types
Explains why the salam commodity must be known and gives rulings for salam in two goods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a valid salam contract and apply them to scenarios involving multiple commodities or mixed capital.

Core Idea
Ignorance of the commodity or capital shares invalidates salam – certainty is required in the object of sale.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq, the commentator explains the necessity of fully knowing the salam commodity (muslam fihi) and the capital (ra's al-mal). The proof is drawn from the practice of Ibn Umar and the principle that a Companion's legal opinion takes precedence over analogy. The text then applies these principles to cases of salam in two commodities or two currencies, showing that incomplete specification leads to invalidity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling and its basis
The passage first asserts that the salam commodity must be known. Identify the rational justification (potential inability to deliver) and the textual proof (Ibn Umar's statement and the priority of Companion's opinion over qiyas).
Note that the proof is a Companion's saying, not a direct Quran or hadith, but it is given precedence.
2
Examine the exception for cloth capital
When the capital is cloth (a measured item), a discrepancy in measurement does not reduce the salam commodity but gives the buyer an option. Understand why: because measurement is a description, not a determinant of the sold item's quantity.
This exception highlights that not all measurement errors affect the contract; only those integral to the commodity's definition.
3
Apply to salam in two commodities
If salam is made in two types (e.g., wheat and barley) without allocating the capital share for each, the contract is invalid. Reason: the capital divides by value, and value is estimated, introducing uncertainty.
The key is that the capital must be clearly assigned to each type; otherwise, jahalah (ignorance) invalidates.
4
Apply to salam with mixed currency capital
If the capital consists of two currencies (dirhams and dinars) against a known quantity of wheat, and one currency amount is unspecified, the entire salam fails. Reason: ignorance of the capital share for the unspecified currency renders the contract fasid.
The unity of the transaction (ittihad al-safqah) means partial invalidity spreads to the whole.
5
Understand the concept of 'counted' (madud)
The passage clarifies that 'counted' items are those whose individual units do not differ, so the contract attaches to quantity. This affects what can be sold via salam.
This is a technical definition that limits salam to fungible goods (mithliyyat).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سمى فيجب التحرز عن مثله وإن كان موهوماً لشرعه من المنافي إذ هو بيع المعدوم، والأولى أن يعلل للإمام بأنه ربما لا يقدر على تحصيل المسلم فيه فيحتاج إلى رد رأس المال فيجب أن يكون معلوماً. وأما ما ذكروه فمندفع بما قدمناه من أن الانتقاد شرط عنده، وقد قال بقول ابن عمر رضي الله عنهما وقول الفقيه من الصحابة مقدم على القياس بخلاف ما إذا كان رأس المال ثوباً لأن الذرع وصف فيه والمبيع لا يقابل الأوصاف فلا يتعلق العقد بقدره، ولذا لو عدد الذرعان فوجده المسلم إليه أنقص لا ينتقص من المسلم فيه شيء وإنما يخير المسلم إليه. ومن فروع المسألة إذا أسلم في جنسين ولم يبين رأس مال أحدهما بأن أسلم مائة في كر حنطة وشعير ولم يبين حصة واحد منهما من رأس المال لم يصح فيهما لأنه ينقسم عليهما باعتبار القيمة وهي تعرف بالحزر أو أسلم جنسين ولم يبين قدر أحدهما بأن أسلم دراهم ودنانير في مقدار معلوم من البر فبين قدر أحدهما ولم يبين الآخر لم يصح السلم فيهما لبطلان العقد في حصته ما لم يعلم قدره فيبطل في الآخر أيضاً لاتحاد الصفقة أو لجهالة حصة الآخر من المسلم فيه فيكون المسلم فيه مجهولاً، والمراد بالمعدود هنا ما لا يتفاوت آحاده لتعلق العقد بمقداره .
Therefore caution must be taken against such a thing even if it is imaginary, because legislation of it is contrary to the law since it is a sale of a non-existent thing. The better view is that the Imam (Abu Hanifa) should explain it as: perhaps he cannot acquire the salam commodity and needs to return the capital, so it must be known. As for what they mentioned, it is refuted by what we have presented: that scrutiny of the capital is a condition according to him, and he adopted the statement of Ibn Umar (may Allah be pleased with them), and the statement of a jurist among the Companions is given precedence over analogy. Unlike when the capital is a cloth, because measuring is a description in it, and the sold item is not subject to descriptions, so the contract does not attach to its quantity. Hence if he counts the measures and finds the quantity less, nothing is deducted from the salam commodity, and the one to whom salam is made has a choice. Among the branches of this issue: if one makes salam in two types without specifying the capital for each, such as giving one hundred in a kurr of wheat and barley without stating the share of each from the capital, the salam is invalid in both because it divides between them according to value, and value is known by estimation. Alternatively, if one makes salam in two currencies (dirhams and dinars) against a known quantity of wheat, and specifies the amount of one but not the other, the salam in both is invalid due to the invalidity of the contract in his share as long as its amount is unknown, so it becomes invalid in the other as well due to the unity of the transaction or due to ignorance of the other's share of the salam commodity, rendering the salam commodity unknown. The meaning of 'counted' here is that whose individual units do not differ, because the contract is attached to its quantity.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining the necessity of knowing the salam commodity. The author states that Imam Abu Hanifa requires the salam commodity to be fully known because it is a sale of a future good (ma'dum), and if the seller cannot deliver, he must return the capital; ignorance leads to disputes. The proof is the saying of Ibn Umar, and the principle that a Companion's legal opinion takes precedence over analogy. The passage then distinguishes the case where the capital is a cloth: since cloth is measured by length, a discrepancy does not reduce the salam commodity but gives an option. Finally, it provides branches: if salam is made in two types (e.g., wheat and barley) without allocating the capital share for each, the contract is invalid because the distribution by value is uncertain. Similarly, if the capital consists of two types (e.g., dirhams and dinars) and one is left unspecified, the entire salam fails due to jahalah (ignorance) affecting the transaction's unity.

Key Terms
السلم (al-salam): A forward sale with immediate payment and deferred delivery of a specified commodity.
المسلم فيه (al-muslam fihi): The commodity to be delivered later in a salam contract, which must be fully known by type, quantity, and quality.
رأس المال (ra's al-mal): The capital paid immediately in a salam contract; must be known and paid at the contract session.
جهالة (jahalah): Ignorance or uncertainty about the subject matter or price, which invalidates the contract in this context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person makes salam in wheat and barley for 100 dirhams without allocating how much of the capital goes to each, what happens?
A) The contract is valid and the capital is divided equally.
B) The contract is invalid because the share of each is unknown.
C) The contract is valid if the value is estimated.
D) The buyer receives wheat only.

Key Takeaway
For a valid salam contract, both the commodity and the capital must be fully specified; any uncertainty invalidates the contract.

Specifying Delivery Place in Salam for Heavy Goods: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on delivery place condition for salam heavy goods, with extensions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for delivery place in salam for heavy goods according to Imam Abu Hanifa, explain the Sahibayn's opposing view, and derive the ruling when place is unspecified.

Core Idea
For heavy goods, Salam delivery place must be specified; without it the contract is fasid per Abu Hanifa.

In salam contracts, the place of delivery can be critical when the goods are heavy and costly to transport. The Hanafi school has an internal dispute: Imam Abu Hanifa requires explicit specification, while his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, consider it unnecessary, relying on the contract location. This lesson unpacks their proofs and concludes with the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition and its scope
Locate the statement about 'place of delivery for things that have a burden'. Understand that this condition applies only to goods with transport cost (haml wa mu'nah).
2
State the dispute between Imam and Sahibayn
Abu Hanifa holds the condition necessary; without it the contract is invalid. The Sahibayn say the contract location suffices, making the contract valid even if unspecified.
3
Analyze each party's proof
Imam's proof: ignorance of location leads to dispute. Sahibayn's proof: analogy to loans and specific sales where the place of obligation is the contract location. The Hanafi response distinguishes salam as deferred delivery.
4
Determine the thamarah and mufta bihi
The practical consequence: if unspecified, according to Imam the contract is fasid; according to Sahibayn it is sahih and delivery is at contract place. The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view.

Commentary
This passage is from the commentary explaining the condition of specifying the place of delivery for salam commodities that require transport (have weight/burden). Imam Abu Hanifa requires this specification, while the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) do not, analogizing to loans and specific sales where the place of obligation is the contract location. The text then includes a super-commentary discussing a previous point about scrutinizing the capital, indicating that the condition of knowledge applies to non-currency capital. Finally, it extends the ikhtilaf to the price in sales, rent, and division, and discusses conditions about delivery at home and carriage, with different opinions about their validity. The relied-upon position is that stipulating delivery at a specific place is valid, but adding carriage after delivery may invalidate the contract due to blending two transactions.

Key Terms
الإيفاء (al-ifāʾ): Delivery or fulfillment of the salam commodity at the agreed place.
الحمل (al-ḥaml): The weight or burden of an item that requires transport cost.
المؤنة (al-muʾnah): Expense or cost associated with transporting the item.
التحالف (al-taḥāluf): Mutual oath taking when parties disagree in a contract; here relevant if place is disputed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The buyer can choose any place.
D) The seller delivers at his own place.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, for salam contracts on goods with transport cost, the place of delivery must be specified; otherwise the contract is invalid (fasid) according to the relied-upon position.

The Place of Delivery in Salam: Abu Hanifa vs. the Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on delivery place in Salam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the specification of the delivery place in a Salam contract, explain the rationale behind each position, and identify the practical consequence (al-tahāluf) in case of dispute.

Core Idea
Delivery place in Salam must be specified—Abu Hanifa requires it; Sahibayn hold contract place suffices.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā'iq presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether the place of delivery must be explicitly stated in a Salam contract. Abu Hanifa insists on specification to avoid disputes over differing values; the Sahibayn consider the contract place sufficient. The discussion extends to analogous contracts like price, rent, and division. Grasping this ikhtilāf sharpens your ability to identify the legal cause (ʿilla) and its effect on contract validity.

Guided Path
1
State the two positions
Identify what Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn rule regarding the delivery place in Salam. Abu Hanifa requires explicit specification; the Sahibayn hold the contract place suffices.
The difference stems from the deferred nature of delivery in Salam.
2
Analyze the rationale (taʿlīl)
Abu Hanifa reasons that because delivery is not immediate, the place of contract is not automatically the place of delivery; values vary by location, so ignorance leads to dispute. The Sahibayn argue that specifying the place is inherent in the contract itself, so the contract place is sufficient.
Contrast with loan and usurpation where the place is automatically specified due to immediate obligation.
3
Identify the practical consequence (thamarah)
According to Abu Hanifa, if the place is not specified, no mutual oath (taḥāluf) occurs; the contract is not binding. According to the Sahibayn, if they dispute, they take mutual oath regarding the claimed terms.
The text states: 'ولا تحالف عنده فيه، وعندهما يتحالفان'.
4
Extend to other contracts
The same dispute applies to the price in a sale when it has transport burden, to rent, and to division of property with burden. In each, Abu Hanifa requires place specification; the Sahibayn default to the contract place.
For goods without burden, different rules apply later.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And they made the mutual oath, according to him: delivery is not obligatory immediately, so the place of the contract is not specified for delivery, unlike loan, usurpation, and consumption, for their delivery is due by the same obligation, so its place is specified. If it is not specified, it remains unknown, an ignorance leading to dispute because values differ by places, so statement is necessary to repel dispute, and it becomes like ignorance of description. Hence some said: difference in place necessitates like difference in description. And it is said: there is no mutual oath according to him in it, but according to them both they take mutual oath because specifying the place is a requirement of the contract. It is restricted to the subject matter of Salam because the place of the contract is specified for delivery of the capital of Salam by agreement. And upon this is the difference regarding the price if it has carriage and burden, and similarly the rent, and division. Its example: they divided a house with a share of one of them that has carriage and burden: according to him, statement of the place of delivery is a condition; according to them both, the place of the contract is specified. And it is said: it is not a condition in the price according to all, and the correct is that it is a condition if it is deferred; and according to them both, the place of the contract is specified. And it is said regarding rent that the place of the house and the place of delivery of the riding animal are specified. Then if he specified a city, it is permissible because despite diversity of its sides, it is like a single spot regarding this ruling due to lack of difference in value. Hence if he hired a riding animal to work upon in the city, he may work in any place he wishes. And it is said: this is if the city is not large; if it is large, reaching its outskirts a league, it is not permissible unless they specify a side of it, because ignorance leads to dispute. And if he stipulated to deliver in his house, it is permissible by istihsan because what is meant is the house at the time of the term's maturity customarily, and the apparent is his remaining in his house. And if he stipulated carriage to his house: it is said it is permissible because it is a stipulation of delivery in it; and it is said it is not permissible because carriage is not required by the contract, only delivery is required, and it is conceivable without carriage, so it is corrupting. And if he stipulated to deliver him in a place then carry it to his house, it is not permissible. The summary: stipulating delivery in a place is validating, and regarding stipulating carriage to a specific place there are two sayings; and stipulating carriage after delivery is corrupting, and its opposite is not, like delivery after delivery; its completion is in al-Khulasa. And in al-Bazzaziyya: stipulating his carriage to the house of the rabb al-salam after delivery in the stipulated place is not correct due to the combination of two transactions, lease and trade. And stipulation of delivery alone, or carriage alone, or delivery after carriage is permissible, not stipulation of delivery after delivery according to the saying of general teachers, like his stipulation to deliver in a district then deliver in his house. And if he stipulated delivery or carriage after al-halum it is not permissible. And in some fawa'id: stipulation of carriage after carriage is correct because carriage does not give ownership to the rabb al-salam; when he stipulated carriage a second time, it is like his statement: (Upon this is the difference regarding the price) i.e., the price of the sold item in sale. His statement: (And if he stipulated delivery or carriage after carriage it is not permissible) some virtuous scholars said: in it is contradiction to his statement: 'or delivery after the preceding carriage'. And in a copy of al-Bazzaziyya: 'and if he stipulated carriage after delivery or carriage etc.' and upon it there is no contradiction, and in it is repetition unless it is taken as emphasis, so reflect. So also I saw in my two copies of al-Bazzaziyya. His statement: (it is not permissible) because in one of the two sides, it became like his stipulating it once; and likewise delivery after carriage and delivery after delivery. When he stipulated that, the first delivery becomes invalid. And if he stipulated delivery in a specific city, then all its districts are equal; even if he delivered in his district, he cannot demand from him in another district. And in Fath al-Qadir: if he bought food with food of its type and one of them stipulated delivery to his house, it is not permissible by consensus however it may be. And if he stipulated to deliver to a specific place and he delivered elsewhere and paid carriage to the stipulated place, he becomes a taker, and taking the carriage is not permissible; if he wishes, he may return it to him to deliver to him in the stipulated place because it is his right. And in al-Bada'i': if he delivered in other than the stipulated place, the rabb al-salam may refuse; if he gives him a wage for that, it is not permissible for him to take the wage, and he may return the subject matter of Salam until he delivers it in the stipulated place, contrary to the shafi' if he is reconciled from it with wealth: it is not valid and his right drops due to his turning away from demand, as if he explicitly drops it; but the right of the rabb al-salam regarding delivery in the stipulated place does not drop by explicit dropping. It is restricted to what has carriage because what does not have carriage, like musk, camphor, saffron, and small pearls, specifying the place of delivery is not a condition. And he restricted it in Fath al-Qadir to being small in quantity; otherwise, it may be delivered in large amounts of saffron reaching a considerable total and he delivers it in the place where he made the Salam. And whenever we say the place of the contract is specified, it is restricted to what delivering is possible there; otherwise, if he made Salam while both are on a ship at sea or a mountain, then it must be in the nearest place that is possible. This is according to the narration of al-Jami' al-Saghir. And it is mentioned in al-Ijarat that what has no carriage may be delivered in any place he wishes, and this is the more correct because all places are equal. And if he specified a place: it is said it is not specified, and it is said it is specified; and so in Fath al-Qadir. And he corrected in al-Muhit that the place of the contract is specified regarding what has no carriage because the more correct is that value differs with difference of place; camphor is more valuable in the city due to greater demand and less in the countryside.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling from the chapter of Salam (advance payment sale) concerning the place of delivery. It opens with an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa holds that since delivery is not immediately required, the place of contract does not automatically become the place of delivery; therefore, the parties must explicitly specify the delivery place to avoid disputes arising from differing values in different locations. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) argue that specifying the place is a necessary implication of the contract itself, so the contract place suffices. The passage then extends this principle to other contracts: the price in a sale if it has transport burden, rent, and division of property. The commentator clarifies that for goods without transport burden (like musk, camphor), the condition of specifying place is relaxed, though some authorities still require it because even light goods may vary in value by location. Detailed rules follow on stipulations: specifying a city is valid, but if the city is large, a district must be named. Stipulating delivery at the seller's house is valid by istihsan. Stipulating carriage after delivery is corrupting, as it combines lease and sale. Stipulating delivery after delivery is also invalid. The passage concludes with practical consequences: if delivery is made in a wrong place, the buyer may refuse or accept with compensation, and his right to demand delivery in the stipulated place does not drop even by explicit waiver. Throughout, the reasoning is based on preventing disputes and on analogy to similar contracts like loan and usurpation.

Key Terms
التحالف (al-tahāluf): The mutual oath taken by both parties when they disagree about a contract term, each swearing to their claim.
السلم (al-salam): An advance payment sale where the price is paid immediately and the commodity is delivered later.
الإيفاء (al-īfā'): Delivery or performance of the subject matter of the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on mutual oath (tahāluf) when the delivery place is not specified in a Salam contract?
A) No mutual oath is taken according to Abu Hanifa; yes according to Sahibayn.
B) Mutual oath is always taken.
C) No mutual oath ever.
D) Mutual oath is taken only if the place is specified.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa requires explicit delivery place in Salam to avoid dispute; the Sahibayn deem the contract place sufficient.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Detailed Rules on Delivery Place: City Size, Carriage, and Goods Without Burden
Detailed rulings on delivery place in Salam and related contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rulings on specifying the delivery place when goods have no transport burden, when the city is large, and when stipulations about carriage are added, and identify which stipulations corrupt the contract.

Core Idea
For goods without burden, place need not be specified; for large cities, a district must be named; stipulating carriage after delivery invalidates the contract.

Continuing from the core dispute on delivery place in Salam, the commentary now addresses specific scenarios and stipulations. The rules depend on whether the goods have transport burden, the size of the city, and the nature of additional carriage conditions. Understanding these details is essential for drafting valid Salam contracts and avoiding corruption (fasād) through forbidden stipulations.

Guided Path
1
Classify goods by transport burden
Identify goods with burden (like grain, cement) and those without (like musk, camphor, small pearls). For goods without burden, specifying the place is not a condition; delivery may occur anywhere.
Even for light goods, some later authorities require specification because value can still vary.
2
Apply the rule for large cities
If a city is large (outskirts exceed a league), naming the city alone is insufficient; a specific district must be mentioned to avoid dispute.
This applies to Salam and also to hiring a riding animal.
3
Distinguish valid and invalid stipulations on carriage
Stipulating delivery at the seller's house is valid (istihsan). Stipulating carriage to a specific place is disputed but generally permissible. Stipulating carriage after delivery is corrupting because it combines two contracts. Stipulating delivery after delivery is also invalid.
The key distinction is whether the stipulation adds a separate obligation beyond the delivery itself.
4
Identify the consequence of delivery in the wrong place
If the seller delivers elsewhere, the buyer may refuse or accept but cannot charge extra for the transport. The buyer's right to demand delivery in the specified place does not drop even by explicit waiver.
Contrast with the case of a shafi' (pre-emptor) where the right drops by waiver.

Key Terms
التحالف (al-tahāluf): Mutual oath between disputing parties in a sale.
السلم (al-salam): Advance payment sale.
المؤنة (al-mūna): Transport burden or expense.
الفساد (al-fasād): Corruption of a contract due to invalid conditions.

The Condition of Receiving Capital Before Separation in Salam
Explaining the ruling on the requirement to receive the capital in Salam before the parties separate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on the requirement to receive the capital in a Salam contract before the parties separate, and distinguish between the condition being for initial validity versus remaining validity.

Core Idea
In Salam, receiving the capital before separation is a condition for the contract to remain valid, not for its initial formation.

In Salam (advance payment sale), the buyer pays the capital immediately in exchange for deferred delivery of a commodity. Because the exchange is immediate (capital) for deferred (commodity), Hanafi jurists require that the capital be received by the seller before the parties leave the contract session. This lesson unpacks that condition, explains its basis in qiyas, and clarifies an important nuance: the contract is not void from the start if capital is not received, but it becomes invalid once the parties separate without receipt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the Salam contract. The ruling is that receiving the capital before the parties separate is a necessary condition for the contract's validity. Identify that this is a shart (condition) in the contract.
2
Determine the nature of the condition
Is this condition for the initial conclusion of the contract (shart in'iqad) or for its continued validity (shart baqa')? The passage notes two views. Identify the difference: initial invalidity vs. invalidity only upon separation.
The correct view is that it is a condition for remaining validity (baqa).
3
Locate the dalil
The proof is qiyas (analogy) to three contracts: hawala (assignment), kafala (suretyship), and sarf (currency exchange). In those contracts, receipt before separation is required for completion.
4
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The effective cause (illa) is that these contracts involve immediate exchange or assurance equivalent, so the capital must be received before the session ends to align the contract with its name and purpose.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Some scholars (qawl al-ba'd) view receipt as a condition for initial validity. The correct view (الصحيح) is that it is a condition for remaining validity. This difference affects whether the contract becomes void immediately or only upon separation.
The commentator explicitly states 'والصحيح أنه شرط بقائه على الصحة'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وقبض رأس المال قبل (الافتراق أي وشرطه قبض رأس المال قبل أن يتفرقا لأن السلم ينبئ عن أخذ عاجل بآجل وذلك بالقبض قبل الافتراق ليكون حكمه على وفق ما يقتضيه اسمه كما في الحوالة والكفالة والصرف. وظاهر كلامه أن القبض شرط انعقاده صحيحاً كبقية الشروط وهو قول البعض، والصحيح أنه شرط بقائه على الصحة فينعقد صحيحاً بدونه ثم يفسد بالافتراق بلا قبض، وستأتي فائدة الاختلاف في الصرف. وأطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان رأس المال مما لا يتعين أو يتعين لما ذكرناه، وفي الخلاصة : ولو أبى المسلم إليه قبض رأس المال أجبر عليه .اهـ. وفي الواقعات باع عبداً بثوب موصوف في الذمة فإن زيادة وهي الحمل شرنبلالية عن المحيط. قوله : (وفي الواقعات باع عبداً بثوب الخ) كان الأولى تقديمه على عبارة الخلاصة لأنه مقابل لما أفاده الإطلاق. وفي فتح القدير وإن كان عيناً ففي القياس لا يشترط تعجيله وفي الاستحسان يشترط .اهـ. فهو مفرع على القياس وفي حاشية أبي السعود عن
His statement: (And receipt of the capital before separation) i.e., its condition is receipt of the capital before they separate, because Salam indicates taking immediate for deferred, and that is by receipt before separation, so that its ruling conforms to what its name requires, as in hawala, kafala, and sarf. And the apparent of his statement is that receipt is a condition for its valid conclusion, like the rest of conditions, and that is the saying of some. And the correct is that it is a condition for its remaining upon validity, so it is validly concluded without it, then becomes invalid by separation without receipt. And the benefit of the difference will come in sarf. And he made it absolute, so it includes whether the capital is of the type that is not specified or is specified, due to what we mentioned. And in al-Khulasa: if the person to whom Salam is given refuses to receive the capital, he is compelled to do so. And in al-Waqi'at: he sold a slave for a cloth described in the dhimma; then an increase, which is the carriage, from al-Shurunbulali from al-Muhit. His statement: (And in al-Waqi'at: he sold a slave for a cloth... etc.) It would have been better to present it before the expression of al-Khulasa because it contrasts with what the absoluteness gives. And in Fath al-Qadir: even if it is a specific asset, by qiyas its immediate (receipt) is not a condition, but by istihsan it is a condition. So it is branched from qiyas. And in Hashiyat Abi al-Sa'ud from...
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage explains the condition that the capital in a Salam contract must be received by the seller before the parties separate from the contract session. The commentator begins by quoting the base text from Kanz al-Daqa'iq: 'And receipt of the capital before separation.' He explains that this condition is necessary because Salam is a forward contract where the price (capital) is paid immediately in exchange for deferred delivery; receiving the capital before separation ensures that the contract fulfills its purpose. He draws analogy to three other contracts: hawala (assignment of debt), kafala (suretyship), and sarf (currency exchange), where immediate receipt before separation is also required. The commentator then clarifies a subtle point: some scholars hold that receipt is a condition for the contract's initial validity, meaning that if the capital is not received before separation, the contract is void from the start. However, the correct view (الصحيح) is that receipt is a condition for the contract's remaining valid: the contract is initially valid even without receipt, but if the parties separate without receipt, the contract then becomes void (fasad). This is an important distinction because if the capital is received before separation, the contract remains valid; if not, it becomes invalid only after separation. The passage also notes that the condition applies whether the capital is a specific object (عين) or a generic obligation. It cites al-Khulasa that the seller can be compelled to accept the capital if he refuses. Finally, it mentions a different opinion from Fath al-Qadir: by qiyas, if the capital is a specific asset, immediate receipt is not required, but by istihsan it is required, indicating that the relied-upon position is the istihsan (the practical ruling).

Key Terms
قبض (qabḍ): Physical or constructive taking possession of the capital (money or goods) in a Salam contract.
الافتراق (al-iftirāq): The separation of the parties from the contract session (majlis al-aqd).
شرط بقاء (shart baqā'): A condition for the continued validity of a contract; if violated, the contract becomes invalid only at the time of violation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the correct view in the Hanafi school, what is the status of a Salam contract if the capital is not received before the parties separate?
A) The contract is void from the beginning.
B) The contract is valid and remains valid.
C) The contract is initially valid but becomes void upon separation.
D) The contract is suspended and becomes valid upon subsequent receipt.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the requirement to receive capital in Salam before separation is a condition for the contract to remain valid, not for its initial formation.
