Stipulating Place of Delivery in Salam
Ruling on stipulating delivery location in salam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on stipulating place of delivery in salam and distinguish between goods with and without carrying capacity.

Core Idea
Stipulating place of delivery in salam is binding; for lightweight goods, the place of contract is specified due to varying values.

In a salam contract, the place of delivery can be stipulated and becomes binding. This affects both bulky and lightweight goods, but the latter have nuanced rules due to value differences across locations. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the conditions and the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being ruled upon? The stipulation of place of delivery in a salam contract. The ruling is that it is valid (sahih) and binding if stipulated.
The contract does not become invalid; the condition is enforceable.
2
Locate the proof (dalil)
The proof is based on qiyas and istihsan: the value of goods varies by location, so specifying place ensures fairness. The passage cites no direct Quran or hadith but relies on customary practice and juristic reasoning.
The dalil is zanni (conjectural) in both transmission and meaning.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The effective cause (illa) is the difference in value due to location. For example, camphor is more valuable in the city than in rural areas, so delivery at the contract place protects the salam buyer's interest.
This reasoning leads to the specification of the place of contract for lightweight goods.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
There is a disagreement within the Hanafi school: according to al-Jami' al-Saghir, the place of contract is specified for goods without carrying; according to al-Ijarat, any place is acceptable. al-Muhit supports specification.
The difference stems from whether the value variation is considered significant.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that the place of contract is specified, as supported by al-Muhit and later scholars like al-Haskafi, because value indeed differs by location.
This is the preferred view for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صار كشرطه مرة وكذا الإيفاء بعد الحمل والإيفاء بعد الإيفاء ولما شرط ذلك صار الإيفاء الأول منفسخاً وإذا شرط الإيفاء في مدينة كذا فكل محلاتها سواء حتى لو أوفاه في محله ليس له أن يطالبه في محلة أخرى اهـ وفي فتح القدير ولو اشترى طعاماً بطعام من جنسه واشترط أحدهما التوفية إلى منزله لم يجز بالإجماع كيفما كان ولو شرط أن يوفيه إلى مكان كذا فسلمه في غيره ودفع الكراء إلى الموضع المشروط صار قابضاً ولا يجوز أخذ الكراء وإن شاء رده إليه ليسلمه إليه في المكان المشروط لأنه حقه اهـ وفي البدائع فإن سلم في غير المكان المشروط فلرب السلم أن يأبى فإن أعطاه على ذلك أجراً لم يجز له أخذ الأجر عليه وله أن يرد المسلم فيه حتى يسلمه في المكان المشروط بخلاف الشفيع إذا صولح عنها بمال لم يصح وسقط حقه لإعراضه عن الطلب كما لو أسقطه صريحاً وحق رب السلم في التسليم في المكان المشروط لم يسقط بالإسقاط صريحاً اهـ قيد بماله حمل لأن ما لا حمل له كالمسك والكافور والزعفران وصغار اللؤلؤ لا يشترط فيه بيان مكان الإيفاء وقيده في فتح القدير بأن يكون قليلاً وإلا فقد يسلم في أمناء من الزعفران كثيرة تبلغ إجمالاً ويسلمه في المكان الذي أسلم فيه وكلما قلنا يتعين مكان العقد فهو مقيد بما إذا كان مما يتأتى فيه التسليم وما لا بأن أسلم إليه وهما في مركب في البحر أو جبل فإنه يجب في أقرب الأماكن التي يمكن فيها وهذا على رواية الجامع الصغير وذكر في الإجارات أن ما لا حمل له يوفيه في أي مكان شاء وهو الأصح لأن الأماكن كلها سواء ولو عين مكاناً قيل لا يتعين وقيل يتعين وهو كذا في فتح القدير وصحح في المحيط أنه يتعين موضع العقد فيما لا حمل له لأن الأصح القيمة تختلف باختلاف الأماكن فالكافور أكثر قيمة في المصر لكثرة الرغبة فيه في المصر وقلتها في السواد اهـ
It became like his stipulation once, and likewise delivery after pregnancy and delivery after delivery. When he stipulated that, the first delivery becomes rescinded. If he stipulates delivery in such-and-such a city, then all its localities are the same, so that if he delivers it in his locality, he does not have the right to demand him in another locality. End quote. In Fath al-Qadir: If one buys food with food of its genus and one of them stipulates delivery to his house, it is not valid by consensus, however it may be. If he stipulates that he deliver it to a certain place, then he delivers it elsewhere and pays the hire to the stipulated place, he becomes a taker and it is not permissible to take the hire. If he wishes, he may return it to him to deliver it to the stipulated place because it is his right. End quote. In al-Bada'i: If he delivers at other than the stipulated place, the owner of the salam may refuse. If he gives him a wage for that, it is not permissible for him to take the wage. He has the right to reject the muslam fihi until he delivers it at the stipulated place, unlike the shafi' if he is settled with property, it is not valid and his right falls due to his turning away from demand, as if he explicitly dropped it. And the right of the owner of the salam to delivery at the stipulated place does not fall by explicit dropping. End quote. It is restricted to what has weight/carrying, because what has no carrying like musk, camphor, saffron, and small pearls, statement of place of delivery is not required. It is restricted in Fath al-Qadir to it being little; otherwise, he may deliver in large bags of saffron amounting to loads and deliver it at the place where he made the salam. Whenever we say the place of contract is specified, it is restricted to what is possible for delivery; if it is not possible, like if he made salam to him while they are on a ship in the sea or a mountain, then it is obligatory in the nearest places possible. This is according to the narration of al-Jami' al-Saghir. And it is mentioned in al-Ijarat that what has no carrying, he may deliver in any place he wishes, and this is the most correct because all places are equal. If he specifies a place, it is said it is not specified, and it is said it is specified; that is in Fath al-Qadir. It is supported in al-Muhit that the place of contract is specified for what has no carrying because the most correct is that value differs by difference of places, for camphor is more valuable in the city due to much desire for it in the city and little desire in rural areas. End quote.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses the ruling on stipulating the place of delivery (makān al-īfā') in a salam (forward sale) contract. The passage begins by noting that if a condition is stipulated regarding delivery, it must be honored. For example, if one stipulates delivery in a particular city, all localities within that city are considered the same; the seller cannot insist on delivery in a different locality within the same city. The passage then cites Fath al-Qadir: if someone buys food of the same genus and stipulates delivery to his house, the contract is invalid by consensus. However, if he stipulates a specific place and then delivers elsewhere, he may pay the hire to the stipulated place to make the delivery valid, but the buyer is not required to accept the hire; he may still demand delivery at the stipulated place. The passage distinguishes between goods that have weight/bulk (mā lahu ḥaml) and those that do not (mā lā ḥamlah). For lightweight goods like musk, camphor, saffron, and small pearls, specifying the place of delivery is not required. However, Fath al-Qadir adds that this applies only if the quantity is small; otherwise (e.g., large amounts of saffron), the place of contract is considered the place of delivery. The passage also discusses a scenario where the parties are on a ship or mountain; then the nearest possible place is required. There is a difference of opinion: according to the narration of al-Jami' al-Saghir, the place of contract is specified; but in al-Ijarat, the correct view is that for goods without carrying capacity, the seller may deliver anywhere. Al-Muhit supports specification because the value of such goods varies by location (e.g., camphor is more valuable in the city due to high demand).

Key Terms
الإيفاء (al-īfā'): Delivery of the muslam fihi (the goods in salam) at the agreed place.
ما لا حمل له (mā lā ḥamla lah): Items that are lightweight and easily portable, such as musk, camphor, saffron, and small pearls.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a salam contract for 5 kg of saffron (lightweight good) with no stipulation on place, according to the preferred Hanafi position, where must delivery occur?
A) Any place the seller chooses.
B) The place of contract.
C) The buyer's residence.
D) The nearest market.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, stipulating place of delivery in salam is binding; for lightweight goods, the place of contract is the default due to value variation.

Defective or Disputed Capital in Salam: General Rulings
General rulings on defective/disputed capital in salam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings for when salam capital is found mustahaqq or defective, distinguishing between 'ain and dayn and between before and after iftiraq.

Core Idea
If salam capital is mustahaqq without ratification, the contract is invalid after separation; if defective and not accepted, invalid after separation.

In a salam contract, the capital (ra's al-mal) must be validly owned and free from defects. The Hanafi school provides detailed rulings for when the capital is discovered to be defective or rightfully owned by another person. These rulings depend on the nature of the capital and the timing of the discovery.

Guided Path
1
Identify the nature of the capital
Determine whether the capital is a specific commodity ('ain) or a debt (dayn). The rules differ accordingly.
2
Apply the mustahaqq rule
If the capital is mustahaqq, check if the rightful owner ratifies the contract. If not, the salam is invalid after separation.
If ratified, valid.
3
Apply the defect rule
If the capital is defective, check if the muslam ilayh accepts the defect. If not, the salam is invalid after separation.
If accepted, valid.
4
Consider the timing
If the discovery is before separation, substitution (istibdal) in the same majlis can save the contract.
After separation, invalidity is final.
5
Determine recourse
The buyer (rabb al-salam) can seek recourse from the original payer (naqid) for the loss.
Recourse in kind or value.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

بدل الصرف على هذه التفاصيل، فإن كان عيناً فوجد مستحقاً أو معيباً فإن لم يجز المستحق ولم يرض المسلم إليه بالعيب بطل السلم بعد الافتراق أو قبله، وإن أجاز المستحق ورضي المسلم إليه بالعيب جاز مطلقاً، وله النصف اهـ وفيه لو وجد البعض نبهرجة أو مستحقة فاختلفا فقال رب السلم هو ثلث رأس المال وقال المسلم إليه نصفه فالقول قول رب السلم مع يمينه، ولو كان ستوقة أو رصاصاً فاختلفا في مثل ذلك فالقول قول المسلم إليه وبيانه فيه اهـ. جعل : قوله : (فإن أسلم مائتي درهم في كر بر مائة دينا عليه ومائة نقداً فالسلم في الدين باطل) أي في حصته لكونه ديناً بدين، وصح في حصة النقد لوجود قبض رأس المال بقدره ولا يشيع الفساد And in Talkhis al-Jami' from the chapter of the sick person's acknowledgment of another heir: if he gives salam then borrows, set-off occurs, but not vice versa. End. That is, set-off does not occur unless they settle mutually, as will be mentioned in Al-Bada'i. And it branches from that qabd is a condition when qabd occurs and then qabd is revoked due to a cause that requires it: that the salam is invalidated. Its explanation is that the capital is either an 'ain (specific commodity) or a dayn (debt), and each of them is either found to be rightfully owned by someone else (mustahaqq) or defective, and each of these is before or after separation, wholly or partially, similarly for currency exchange. If it is an 'ain and found mustahaqq or defective, and if the rightful owner does not ratify and the muslam ilayh is not satisfied with the defect, the salam is invalidated after separation or before. If the rightful owner ratifies and the muslam ilayh is satisfied with the defect, it is valid absolutely. And he has the right to recourse against the payer (naqid) with its equivalent if it is a mithli (fungible) or with its value if it is a qimi (non-fungible). If it is a dayn and he finds it mustahaqq and it is ratified, the salam proceeds absolutely, and the buyer has no recourse against what is received, and he recourse against the payer with its equivalent. If it is not ratified, then if he replaces it in the majlis, it is valid; if after, it is invalid. If he finds them zuyuf (counterfeit), nabhiraja (base), rustuqa (tinny), or rasas (lead) - if they are zuyuf and he is satisfied with them [it is valid] absolutely because they are not of the same genus as his right. If he is not satisfied: if before separation and he replaces in the majlis, valid; if after, invalid according to Imam Abu Hanifa absolutely, whether he replaces in the majlis or not. This if he finds them zuyuf or nabhiraja. If he finds them rustuqa or rasas, then after separation it is invalid whether he tolerates it or not, and if he replaces in the majlis, valid. And complete ramifications are in Al-Bada'i. In Al-Sughra: If the muslam ilayh brings some dirhams and says 'I found them zuyuf', his statement is accepted. End. In Al-Idah: Abu Hanifa practiced istihsan for a small amount, saying he should return them and replace in that majlis. In defining 'much' there are two narrations: what exceeds one-third and what exceeds one-half. End. In it: if he finds some of them nabhiraja or mustahaqq and they differ, the rabb al-salam saying it is one-third of the capital and the muslam ilayh saying it is half, then the statement is of the rabb al-salam with his oath. If they are rustuqa or rasas and they differ similarly, the statement is of the muslam ilayh, and its explanation is therein. End. He made: His statement: 'If he gave salam for two hundred dirhams for a kurr of wheat, with a hundred as a debt owed to him and a hundred in cash, then the salam for the debt is invalid' - meaning in its portion because it is debt for debt, and valid for the cash portion due to the existence of receipt of capital to its extent, and the invalidity does not spread because it is incidental since the salam occurred validly entirely; hence if he paid the entire amount before separation, it is valid. And the restriction that he added the contract to the two hundred is incidental; rather it is so if he added it to two hundred absolutely, then the hundred from the capital are offset by the debt owed to him in the correct view because the meaning combines them, which is that the invalidity is incidental since the debt is not determined by the addition of the contract. And he restricted with 'a debt owed to him' because if he said 'I give salam to you for this hundred and the hundred that is owed to me by so-and-so', it is invalid entirely even if he paid the entire amount, due to the condition of delivery of the price to a non-contracting party, which is a connected invalidating factor, so it spreads. And he restricted that the debt be of the same genus as the currency because if the genus differs, e.g., he has a hundred dirhams owed to him, so he gave salam for it and ten dinars for a known cubic measure, it is not valid entirely. As for the debt, it is apparent; and for the portion of the 'ain, due to ignorance of what pertains to it. This is according to Imam Abu Hanifa. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is valid for the portion of the 'ain, and it is based on the issue of declaring the amount of the capital. And he restricted that he made the debt as capital for him because if he did not make it, but the set-off occurred such that each was obligated...
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage delves into detailed rulings when the capital (ra's al-mal) in a salam contract is found to be defective or belonging to someone else (mustahaqq). It distinguishes between capital that is a specific commodity ('ain) and debt (dayn). For 'ain, if the capital is mustahaqq and the rightful owner does not ratify, or if it is defective and the muslam ilayh (the seller) does not accept, the salam is invalidated. If ratified by the rightful owner and accepted by the muslam ilayh, it is valid, and the buyer can seek recourse from the payer. For dayn, similar rules apply. The text also covers counterfeit coins (zuyuf, nabhiraja, rustuqa, rasas). For zuyuf, if the muslam ilayh is satisfied, it is valid; if not, substitution before separation works; after separation, Imam Abu Hanifa says it is invalid absolutely. For rustuqa and rasas (base metals), after separation it is invalid regardless. The passage includes an istihsan from Abu Hanifa for small amounts (yasir) where the muslam ilayh may return and replace in the same session, with two narrations on what constitutes 'much' (one-third or one-half). A further dispute between the muslam ilayh and rabb al-salam about the quantity of defective coins is resolved: if the defect is zuyuf or mustahaqq, the rabb al-salam's statement is taken with oath; if rustuqa or rasas, the muslam ilayh's statement is taken. Then the sharh begins explaining a case where part of the capital is a debt. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if a person gives salam for 200 dirhams, with 100 being a debt already owed, the salam in the debt portion is invalid (debt for debt). But it is valid for the cash portion because receipt occurs for that part. The invalidity does not spread because it is incidental. If the capital is entirely cash, it is valid. The sharh clarifies restrictions: the debt must be of the same genus as the currency. If different, it is invalid entirely. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad allow validity for the 'ain portion, based on the issue of declaring the amount of capital. The passage ends abruptly with a discussion on set-off.

Key Terms
مستحق (mustahaqq): Property that is rightfully owned by someone other than the payer.
زيوف (zuyuf): Counterfeit coins that are not genuine currency.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if salam capital is a dayn and found mustahaqq and not ratified before separation, what action can save the contract?
A) The contract is already invalid; no action can save it.
B) The buyer can substitute the capital in the same majlis.
C) The seller must accept the defect.
D) The contract proceeds automatically.

Key Takeaway
The validity of a salam contract depends on the capital being free from defects or disputes at the time of separation; before separation, substitution in the same session can restore validity.

Istihsan for Slight Defects and Disputes over Quantity of Defective Coins
Istihsan for slight defects and dispute resolution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply Abu Hanifa's istihsan for slight defects in salam and resolve disputes between the parties regarding the quantity of defective coins.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa allows returning and replacing small amounts of defective coins in the same session; 'small' is defined as one-third or one-half by different narrations.

The Hanafi school introduces istihsan (juristic preference) for minor defects in the capital of a salam contract. Imam Abu Hanifa ruled that if a small amount of defective coins is found, the muslam ilayh may return them and substitute in the same session. This lesson covers that istihsan, the definition of 'small', and procedural rules when the parties disagree on the quantity of defective coins.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defect type
Determine if the defective coins are zuyuf/nabhiraja (counterfeit/base) or rustuqa/rasas (base metals).
Istihsan applies to zuyuf/nabhiraja.
2
Apply the istihsan for yasir
If the defective amount is small (yasir), Abu Hanifa allows returning and replacing in the same majlis.
The definition of kathir (much) is disputed: one-third or one-half.
3
Resolve disputes on quantity
If the parties differ on whether the defective portion is kathir, the ruling depends on the coin type.
For zuyuf/mustahaqq, the buyer's statement with oath is taken; for rustuqa/rasas, the seller's statement is taken.

وفي تلخيص الجامع من باب إقرار المريض لوراث آخر والدينين قضاء لأولهما فلو أسلم ثم استقرض وقعت المقاصصة وفي عكسه لا .اهـ. أي لا تقع المقاصة إلا إذا تقاصا بدليل ما سنذكره عن البدائع ويتفرع على أن القبض شرط ما إذا قبض ثم انتقض القبض لمعنى أوجبه أنه يبطل السلم وبيانه أن رأس المال إما أن يكون عيناً أو ديناً، وكل منهما إما يوجد مستحقاً أو معيباً، وكل إما أن يكون قبل الافتراق أو بعده كله أو بعضه، وكذا بدل الصرف على هذه التفاصيل، فإن كان عيناً فوجد مستحقاً أو معيباً فإن لم يجز المستحق ولم يرض المسلم إليه بالعيب بطل السلم بعد الافتراق أو قبله، وإن أجاز المستحق ورضي المسلم إليه بالعيب جاز مطلقاً، وله أن يرجع على الناقد بمثله إن كان مثلياً أو بقيمته إن كان قيمياً. وإن كان ديناً فإن وجده مستحقاً وأجيز مضى السلم مطلقاً ولا سبيل للمشتري على المقبوض ويرجع على الناقد بمثله، وإن لم يجز فاستبدل في المجلس صح وإن بعده بطل. وإن وجده زيوفاً أو نبهرجة أو ستوقة أو رصاصاً فإن كانت زيوفاً فرضي بها . مطلقاً لأنها ليست من جنس حقه. فإن لم يرض؛ فإن كان قبل الافتراق واستبدل في المجلس صح، وإن بعده بطل عند الإمام مطلقاً، سواء استبدلها في المجلس أو لا. هذا إذا وجدها زيوفاً أو نبهرجة، فإن وجدها ستوقة أو رصاصاً فإن بعد الافتراق بطل سواء تجوز بها أو لا، وإن استبدل في المجلس صح وتمام التفريعات في البدائع وفي الصغرى : المسلم إليه إذا أتى بشيء من الدراهم وقال وجدته زيوفاً فالقول له .اهـ. وفي الإيضاح : استحسن أبو حنيفة في اليسير فقال يردها ويستبدل في ذلك المجلس وفي تحديد الكثير روايتان ما زاد على الثلث وما زاد على النصف اهـ وفيه لو وجد البعض نبهرجة أو مستحقة فاختلفا فقال رب السلم هو ثلث رأس المال وقال المسلم إليه نصفه فالقول قول رب السلم مع يمينه، ولو كان ستوقة أو رصاصاً فاختلفا في مثل ذلك فالقول قول المسلم إليه وبيانه فيه اهـ. جعل : قوله : (فإن أسلم مائتي درهم في كر بر مائة دينا عليه ومائة نقداً فالسلم في الدين باطل) أي في حصته لكونه ديناً بدين، وصح في حصة النقد لوجود قبض رأس المال بقدره ولا يشيع الفساد لأنه طارئ إذ السلم وقع صحيحاً في الكل ولذا لو نقد الكل قبل الافتراق صح. والتقييد بكونه أضاف العقد إلى المائتين اتفاقي بل كذلك إذا أضافه إلى مائتين مطلقاً ثم المائة من رأس المال قصاصاً بما في ذمته من الدين في الصحيح لأن المعنى يجمعهما وهو كون الفساد طارئاً إذ الدين لا يتعين بإضافة العقد إليه . وقيد بقوله «ديناً عليه» لأنه لو قال أسلمت إليك هذه المائة والمائة التي لي على فلان يبطل في الكل وإن نقد الكل لاشتراط تسليم الثمن على غير العاقد وهو مفسد مقارن فتعدى . وقيد بكون الدين من جنس النقد لأن الجنس لو اختلف بأن كان له على آخر مائة درهم فأسلمها إليه وعشرة دنانير في أكرار معلومة لم يجز في الكل أما الدين فظاهر وأما عدم حصة العين فلجهالة ما يخصه. وهذا عند الإمام رحمه الله تعالى، وعندهما يجوز في حصة العين وهي مبنية على مسألة إعلام قدر رأس المال . وقيد بكونه جعل الدين عليه رأس المال لأنه لو لم يجعله وإنما وقعت المقاصة بأن وجب على
And in Talkhis al-Jami' from the chapter of the sick person's acknowledgment of another heir: if he gives salam then borrows, set-off occurs, but not vice versa. End. That is, set-off does not occur unless they settle mutually, as will be mentioned in Al-Bada'i. And it branches from that qabd is a condition when qabd occurs and then qabd is revoked due to a cause that requires it: that the salam is invalidated. Its explanation is that the capital is either an 'ain (specific commodity) or a dayn (debt), and each of them is either found to be rightfully owned by someone else (mustahaqq) or defective, and each of these is before or after separation, wholly or partially, similarly for currency exchange. If it is an 'ain and found mustahaqq or defective, and if the rightful owner does not ratify and the muslam ilayh is not satisfied with the defect, the salam is invalidated after separation or before. If the rightful owner ratifies and the muslam ilayh is satisfied with the defect, it is valid absolutely. And he has the right to recourse against the payer (naqid) with its equivalent if it is a mithli (fungible) or with its value if it is a qimi (non-fungible). If it is a dayn and he finds it mustahaqq and it is ratified, the salam proceeds absolutely, and the buyer has no recourse against what is received, and he recourse against the payer with its equivalent. If it is not ratified, then if he replaces it in the majlis, it is valid; if after, it is invalid. If he finds them zuyuf (counterfeit), nabhiraja (base), rustuqa (tinny), or rasas (lead) - if they are zuyuf and he is satisfied with them [it is valid] absolutely because they are not of the same genus as his right. If he is not satisfied: if before separation and he replaces in the majlis, valid; if after, invalid according to Imam Abu Hanifa absolutely, whether he replaces in the majlis or not. This if he finds them zuyuf or nabhiraja. If he finds them rustuqa or rasas, then after separation it is invalid whether he tolerates it or not, and if he replaces in the majlis, valid. And complete ramifications are in Al-Bada'i. In Al-Sughra: If the muslam ilayh brings some dirhams and says 'I found them zuyuf', his statement is accepted. End. In Al-Idah: Abu Hanifa practiced istihsan for a small amount, saying he should return them and replace in that majlis. In defining 'much' there are two narrations: what exceeds one-third and what exceeds one-half. End. In it: if he finds some of them nabhiraja or mustahaqq and they differ, the rabb al-salam saying it is one-third of the capital and the muslam ilayh saying it is half, then the statement is of the rabb al-salam with his oath. If they are rustuqa or rasas and they differ similarly, the statement is of the muslam ilayh, and its explanation is therein. End. He made: His statement: 'If he gave salam for two hundred dirhams for a kurr of wheat, with a hundred as a debt owed to him and a hundred in cash, then the salam for the debt is invalid' - meaning in its portion because it is debt for debt, and valid for the cash portion due to the existence of receipt of capital to its extent, and the invalidity does not spread because it is incidental since the salam occurred validly entirely; hence if he paid the entire amount before separation, it is valid. And the restriction that he added the contract to the two hundred is incidental; rather it is so if he added it to two hundred absolutely, then the hundred from the capital are offset by the debt owed to him in the correct view because the meaning combines them, which is that the invalidity is incidental since the debt is not determined by the addition of the contract. And he restricted with 'a debt owed to him' because if he said 'I give salam to you for this hundred and the hundred that is owed to me by so-and-so', it is invalid entirely even if he paid the entire amount, due to the condition of delivery of the price to a non-contracting party, which is a connected invalidating factor, so it spreads. And he restricted that the debt be of the same genus as the currency because if the genus differs, e.g., he has a hundred dirhams owed to him, so he gave salam for it and ten dinars for a known cubic measure, it is not valid entirely. As for the debt, it is apparent; and for the portion of the 'ain, due to ignorance of what pertains to it. This is according to Imam Abu Hanifa. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is valid for the portion of the 'ain, and it is based on the issue of declaring the amount of the capital. And he restricted that he made the debt as capital for him because if he did not make it, but the set-off occurred such that each was obligated...
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage delves into detailed rulings when the capital (ra's al-mal) in a salam contract is found to be defective or belonging to someone else (mustahaqq). It distinguishes between capital that is a specific commodity ('ain) and debt (dayn). For 'ain, if the capital is mustahaqq and the rightful owner does not ratify, or if it is defective and the muslam ilayh (the seller) does not accept, the salam is invalidated. If ratified by the rightful owner and accepted by the muslam ilayh, it is valid, and the buyer can seek recourse from the payer. For dayn, similar rules apply. The text also covers counterfeit coins (zuyuf, nabhiraja, rustuqa, rasas). For zuyuf, if the muslam ilayh is satisfied, it is valid; if not, substitution before separation works; after separation, Imam Abu Hanifa says it is invalid absolutely. For rustuqa and rasas (base metals), after separation it is invalid regardless. The passage includes an istihsan from Abu Hanifa for small amounts (yasir) where the muslam ilayh may return and replace in the same session, with two narrations on what constitutes 'much' (one-third or one-half). A further dispute between the muslam ilayh and rabb al-salam about the quantity of defective coins is resolved: if the defect is zuyuf or mustahaqq, the rabb al-salam's statement is taken with oath; if rustuqa or rasas, the muslam ilayh's statement is taken. Then the sharh begins explaining a case where part of the capital is a debt. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if a person gives salam for 200 dirhams, with 100 being a debt already owed, the salam in the debt portion is invalid (debt for debt). But it is valid for the cash portion because receipt occurs for that part. The invalidity does not spread because it is incidental. If the capital is entirely cash, it is valid. The sharh clarifies restrictions: the debt must be of the same genus as the currency. If different, it is invalid entirely. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad allow validity for the 'ain portion, based on the issue of declaring the amount of capital. The passage ends abruptly with a discussion on set-off.

Key Terms
زيوف (zuyuf): Counterfeit coins that are not genuine currency.
نبهرجة (nabhiraja): Base coins of inferior quality.
ستوقة (rustuqa): Tin coins or base metal coins.
رصاص (rasas): Lead coins.
مستحق (mustahaqq): Property that is rightfully owned by another.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference; here used by Abu Hanifa to allow substitution for a small defect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, how is a dispute resolved when the parties differ on the quantity of defective coins that are rustuqa (tin)?
A) The buyer's statement is taken with oath.
B) The seller's statement is taken.
C) The court decides.
D) The contract is void.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa's istihsan for slight defects allows the contract to be saved, but the definition of 'slight' is disputed, and the resolution of quantity disputes depends on the type of defective coins.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Salam When Part of Capital is a Debt
Intra-Hanafi dispute on mixed capital in salam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between Imam Abu Hanifa's and the Sahibayn's positions on the validity of a salam contract when part of the capital is a debt owed by the muslam ilayh.

Core Idea
Imam Abu Hanifa rules the debt portion invalid (debt for debt) but the cash portion valid; the Sahibayn validate the cash portion entirely.

A salam contract requires the capital to be received before separation. When part of the capital is a debt already owed by the seller to the buyer, a question arises: is this valid? Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the debt portion constitutes a forbidden debt-for-debt sale, while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) consider the cash portion valid. This lesson explores the dispute and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mixed capital scenario
The buyer pays 200 dirhams for a salam, but 100 dirhams is a pre-existing debt owed by the seller to the buyer.
2
Apply Imam Abu Hanifa's position
He rules the debt portion invalid (debt for debt), but the cash portion valid. Invalidity does not spread.
If the entire capital was paid in cash before separation, it is valid.
3
Apply the Sahibayn's position
They validate the cash portion, considering it valid based on i'lam qadr ra's al-mal.
Their view is based on a different usul principle.
4
Identify practical consequence
Under Abu Hanifa, the buyer must separate the debt portion into a different contract; under the Sahibayn, the single contract can proceed for the cash portion.

And in Talkhis al-Jami' from the chapter of the sick person's acknowledgment of another heir: if he gives salam then borrows, set-off occurs, but not vice versa. End. That is, set-off does not occur unless they settle mutually, as will be mentioned in Al-Bada'i. And it branches from that qabd is a condition when qabd occurs and then qabd is revoked due to a cause that requires it: that the salam is invalidated. Its explanation is that the capital is either an 'ain (specific commodity) or a dayn (debt), and each of them is either found to be rightfully owned by someone else (mustahaqq) or defective, and each of these is before or after separation, wholly or partially, similarly for currency exchange. If it is an 'ain and found mustahaqq or defective, and if the rightful owner does not ratify and the muslam ilayh is not satisfied with the defect, the salam is invalidated after separation or before. If the rightful owner ratifies and the muslam ilayh is satisfied with the defect, it is valid absolutely. And he has the right to recourse against the payer (naqid) with its equivalent if it is a mithli (fungible) or with its value if it is a qimi (non-fungible). If it is a dayn and he finds it mustahaqq and it is ratified, the salam proceeds absolutely, and the buyer has no recourse against what is received, and he recourse against the payer with its equivalent. If it is not ratified, then if he replaces it in the majlis, it is valid; if after, it is invalid. If he finds them zuyuf (counterfeit), nabhiraja (base), rustuqa (tinny), or rasas (lead) - if they are zuyuf and he is satisfied with them [it is valid] absolutely because they are not of the same genus as his right. If he is not satisfied: if before separation and he replaces in the majlis, valid; if after, invalid according to Imam Abu Hanifa absolutely, whether he replaces in the majlis or not. This if he finds them zuyuf or nabhiraja. If he finds them rustuqa or rasas, then after separation it is invalid whether he tolerates it or not, and if he replaces in the majlis, valid. And complete ramifications are in Al-Bada'i. In Al-Sughra: If the muslam ilayh brings some dirhams and says 'I found them zuyuf', his statement is accepted. End. In Al-Idah: Abu Hanifa practiced istihsan for a small amount, saying he should return them and replace in that majlis. In defining 'much' there are two narrations: what exceeds one-third and what exceeds one-half. End. In it: if he finds some of them nabhiraja or mustahaqq and they differ, the rabb al-salam saying it is one-third of the capital and the muslam ilayh saying it is half, then the statement is of the rabb al-salam with his oath. If they are rustuqa or rasas and they differ similarly, the statement is of the muslam ilayh, and its explanation is therein. End. He made: His statement: 'If he gave salam for two hundred dirhams for a kurr of wheat, with a hundred as a debt owed to him and a hundred in cash, then the salam for the debt is invalid' - meaning in its portion because it is debt for debt, and valid for the cash portion due to the existence of receipt of capital to its extent, and the invalidity does not spread because it is incidental since the salam occurred validly entirely; hence if he paid the entire amount before separation, it is valid. And the restriction that he added the contract to the two hundred is incidental; rather it is so if he added it to two hundred absolutely, then the hundred from the capital are offset by the debt owed to him in the correct view because the meaning combines them, which is that the invalidity is incidental since the debt is not determined by the addition of the contract. And he restricted with 'a debt owed to him' because if he said 'I give salam to you for this hundred and the hundred that is owed to me by so-and-so', it is invalid entirely even if he paid the entire amount, due to the condition of delivery of the price to a non-contracting party, which is a connected invalidating factor, so it spreads. And he restricted that the debt be of the same genus as the currency because if the genus differs, e.g., he has a hundred dirhams owed to him, so he gave salam for it and ten dinars for a known cubic measure, it is not valid entirely. As for the debt, it is apparent; and for the portion of the 'ain, due to ignorance of what pertains to it. This is according to Imam Abu Hanifa. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is valid for the portion of the 'ain, and it is based on the issue of declaring the amount of the capital. And he restricted that he made the debt as capital for him because if he did not make it, but the set-off occurred such that each was obligated...
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage delves into detailed rulings when the capital (ra's al-mal) in a salam contract is found to be defective or belonging to someone else (mustahaqq). It distinguishes between capital that is a specific commodity ('ain) and debt (dayn). For 'ain, if the capital is mustahaqq and the rightful owner does not ratify, or if it is defective and the muslam ilayh (the seller) does not accept, the salam is invalidated. If ratified by the rightful owner and accepted by the muslam ilayh, it is valid, and the buyer can seek recourse from the payer. For dayn, similar rules apply. The text also covers counterfeit coins (zuyuf, nabhiraja, rustuqa, rasas). For zuyuf, if the muslam ilayh is satisfied, it is valid; if not, substitution before separation works; after separation, Imam Abu Hanifa says it is invalid absolutely. For rustuqa and rasas (base metals), after separation it is invalid regardless. The passage includes an istihsan from Abu Hanifa for small amounts (yasir) where the muslam ilayh may return and replace in the same session, with two narrations on what constitutes 'much' (one-third or one-half). A further dispute between the muslam ilayh and rabb al-salam about the quantity of defective coins is resolved: if the defect is zuyuf or mustahaqq, the rabb al-salam's statement is taken with oath; if rustuqa or rasas, the muslam ilayh's statement is taken. Then the sharh begins explaining a case where part of the capital is a debt. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if a person gives salam for 200 dirhams, with 100 being a debt already owed, the salam in the debt portion is invalid (debt for debt). But it is valid for the cash portion because receipt occurs for that part. The invalidity does not spread because it is incidental. If the capital is entirely cash, it is valid. The sharh clarifies restrictions: the debt must be of the same genus as the currency. If different, it is invalid entirely. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad allow validity for the 'ain portion, based on the issue of declaring the amount of capital. The passage ends abruptly with a discussion on set-off.

Key Terms
دين (dayn): A debt or obligation owed by one party to another.
إعلام قدر رأس المال (i'lam qadr ra's al-mal): Declaring the amount of the capital; a principle used by the Sahibayn to validate the cash portion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if part of the salam capital is a debt owed by the muslam ilayh, what is the ruling on the debt portion?
A) It is valid as long as the amount is known.
B) It is invalid because it is debt-for-debt.
C) It is valid only if the parties agree.
D) The entire contract is invalid.

Key Takeaway
In a salam contract, using a pre-existing debt as part of the capital is invalid for that portion (debt-for-debt), but the cash portion remains valid, and the invalidity does not spread to the entire contract.

Salam with Mixed Capital: Debt and Cash
Ruling and intra-Hanafi dispute on salam with mixed capital

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on salam when the capital is partly a debt and partly cash, and explain the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
In salam, a debt portion invalidates only that share; the cash portion remains valid unless the debt is from a third party or of a different type.

This lesson examines a nuanced situation in salam contracts: when the advance payment (ra's al-mal) is a mix of a debt owed to the seller and ready cash. While the debt portion is invalid as dayn bi dayn, the cash portion remains valid. The Hanafi masters differ on the extent of validity when the capital is specified, and we will unpack the reasoning behind each position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contractual scenario
Understand the setup: the buyer pays 200 dirhams for a load of wheat (kurr of bar), with 100 dirhams being a debt the seller owes him and 100 dirhams in cash.
The debt is owed by the seller (the party receiving the capital) – this is crucial.
2
Determine the ruling on each portion
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the debt portion is invalid because it constitutes a sale of debt for debt (dayn bi dayn). The cash portion is valid because the capital was received in the session.
The invalidity is restricted to the debt share only.
3
Apply the principle of supervening defects (tari')
The invalid portion does not invalidate the whole because the defect is supervening: the contract was initially valid for the entire capital, and the invalidity only arose due to the mix.
If it were a concurrent defect (e.g., capital from a third party), the whole would be invalid.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi difference
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) hold that if the debt is specified as a specific item ('ayn), the entire contract is valid because the capital is known. This is based on a different view of the 'notification of the amount of capital' (iʿlām qadr ra's al-māl).
The relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن أسلم مائتي درهم في كر بر مائة ديناً عليه ومائة نقداً فالسلم في الدين باطل جعل : قوله : (فإن أسلم مائتي درهم في كر بر مائة ديناً عليه ومائة نقداً فالسلم في الدين باطل) أي في حصته لكونه ديناً بدين، وصح في حصة النقد لوجود قبض رأس المال بقدره ولا يشيع الفساد لأنه طارئ إذ السلم وقع صحيحاً في الكل ولذا لو نقد الكل قبل الافتراق صح. والتقييد بكونه أضاف العقد إلى المائتين اتفاقي بل كذلك إذا أضافه إلى مائتين مطلقاً ثم المائة من رأس المال قصاصاً بما في ذمته من الدين في الصحيح لأن المعنى يجمعهما وهو كون الفساد طارئاً إذ الدين لا يتعين بإضافة العقد إليه . وقيد بقوله «ديناً عليه» لأنه لو قال أسلمت إليك هذه المائة والمائة التي لي على فلان يبطل في الكل وإن نقد الكل لاشتراط تسليم الثمن على غير العاقد وهو مفسد مقارن فتعدى . وقيد بكون الدين من جنس النقد لأن الجنس لو اختلف بأن كان له على آخر مائة درهم فأسلمها إليه وعشرة دنانير في أكرار معلومة لم يجز في الكل أما الدين فظاهر وأما عدم حصة العين فلجهالة ما يخصه. وهذا عند الإمام رحمه الله تعالى، وعندهما يجوز في حصة العين وهي مبنية على مسألة إعلام قدر رأس المال . وقيد بكونه جعل الدين عليه رأس المال لأنه لو لم يجعله وإنما وقعت المقاصة بأن وجب على كل عين جعلت رأس مال السلم
If he pays two hundred dirhams for a load of wheat (kurr of bar), one hundred being a debt owed by him and one hundred ready cash, then the salam in the portion of the debt is invalid. [Commentary:] His statement (may God have mercy on him): (If he pays two hundred dirhams for a load of wheat, one hundred being a debt owed by him and one hundred ready cash, then the salam in the portion of the debt is invalid) meaning in its share because it is a debt for a debt, and it is valid in the share of the cash due to the existence of receipt of the capital to its extent, and the invalidity does not spread because it is supervening, since the salam occurred validly in the whole; that is why if he pays the whole before parting, it is valid. The restriction that he added the contract to two hundred is incidental; rather it is likewise if he adds it absolutely to two hundred, then the one hundred from the capital is set off against the debt he owes, in the sound view, because the meaning combines them, which is the defect being supervening, since the debt is not specified by adding the contract to it. He restricted by saying 'a debt owed by him' because if he says 'I have advanced to you this one hundred and the one hundred that so-and-so owes me,' the whole becomes invalid even if he pays all cash, due to stipulating delivery of the price from other than the contracting party, which is a concurrent corrupting factor, so it becomes excessive. He restricted the debt to be of the same type as the cash because if the types differ, such as if he owed another one hundred dirhams and ten dinars for known loads, the whole is invalid; as for the debt, it is apparent, and as for the non-validity of the portion of the specific item, it is due to ignorance of what is allocated to it. This is according to Imam Abu Hanifa (may God have mercy on him). According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is valid in the share of the specific item, based on the issue of informing the amount of capital. He restricted it to his making the debt the capital because if he did not make it so, but rather a set-off occurred such that each specific item was made capital of the salam.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the ruling on a salam contract where the capital is partly a debt (dayn) and partly cash (naqd). The passage type is a ruling with an intra-Hanafi dispute. The author first states that if someone pays 200 dirhams for a load of wheat (kurr of bar), with 100 being a debt owed to him and 100 being cash, the salam is invalid in the portion of the debt because it constitutes a sale of debt for debt (dayn bi dayn), which is prohibited. However, the cash portion is valid because the capital was received in the session. The invalidity does not spread to the whole because it is supervening (tari'), as the contract was initially sound for the entire capital; if he had paid all cash before parting, the contract would be valid. The commentator then discusses several qualifications (qayd): (1) The specification that the contract was added to 200 dirhams is not exclusive; the same ruling applies if the contract is made absolutely for 200, and then 100 is set off against an existing debt, because the rationale (al-ma'na) is the same: the defect is supervening. (2) The phrase 'a debt owed by him' shows that if instead he says 'I have transferred to you this 100 and the 100 that so-and-so owes me,' the whole contract becomes invalid even if he pays all cash, because it requires delivery from a third party, which is a concurrent defect. (3) The debt must be of the same type as the cash; if the types differ, e.g., 100 dirhams and 10 dinars, the whole contract is invalid. The commentator then notes that this is the position of Imam Abu Hanifa. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (al-Shaybani), the contract is valid in the portion of the specific item (ʿayn) because the amount allocated to it is known by specification, relying on the principle of 'notification of the amount of capital' (iʿlām qadr raʾs al-māl). The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) according to the text is that of Abu Hanifa, as the commentary favors it with detailed reasoning.

Key Terms
السلم (al-salam): A forward sale where payment is made in advance for specified goods to be delivered later.
دين (dayn): A debt; something owed that is not a specific physical entity.
طارئ (tari'): Supervening; a defect that occurs after a valid contract does not spread to invalidate the entire contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on a salam contract where the capital includes a debt owed by the seller?
A) Entirely invalid.
B) Valid in both portions.
C) Invalid in the debt portion, valid in the cash portion.
D) Valid only if the debt is specified as a specific item.

Key Takeaway
In a mixed-capital salam, the invalidity of the debt portion does not spread to the cash portion because it is supervening, not concurrent.

Ruling on Counterfeit and Mixed Capital in Salam
Rulings on counterfeit and mixed capital defects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when salam capital is found to be counterfeit (zuyuf) or mixed (nabahrajah), distinguishing between acceptance, replacement before separation, and the consequence after separation.

Core Idea
Counterfeit capital in salam: accepting is permissible; if rejected and replaced before separation, valid; after separation, void per Imam Abu Hanifa.

In a salam contract, the capital must be delivered as specified. If the buyer finds the capital to be counterfeit (zuyuf) or mixed with base metal (nabahrajah), the validity of the contract depends on acceptance and timing. This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq outlines the implications of these defects.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defect type
Classify the capital as zuyuf (counterfeit) or nabahrajah (mixed with base metal). The ruling applies to both.
The initial list includes stolen and lead, but their rulings are separate.
2
Assess buyer acceptance
If the buyer accepts the defective capital, the contract is valid absolutely because counterfeit coins are not of the same genus as the genuine right.
Acceptance is a voluntary transaction.
3
Rejection with replacement before separation
If the buyer does not accept but replaces the capital with genuine currency in the same session (majlis), the contract remains valid.
The replacement must occur before the parties leave the contract session.
4
Rejection after separation
If the buyer rejects after the parties have separated from the contract session, the contract becomes invalid according to Imam Abu Hanifa, even if replacement occurs later.
This is the mufta bihi position of the Hanafi school.
5
Note the scope
These rulings apply specifically to zuyuf and nabahrajah; stolen and lead capital have different rules covered in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن وجده زيوفاً أو نبهرجة أو ستوقة أو رصاصاً فإن كانت زيوفاً فرضي بها . مطلقاً لأنها ليست من جنس حقه. فإن لم يرض؛ فإن كان قبل الافتراق واستبدل في المجلس صح، وإن بعده بطل عند الإمام مطلقاً، سواء استبدلها في المجلس أو لا. هذا إذا وجدها زيوفاً أو نبهرجة، فإن وجدها ستوقة أو رصاصاً فإن بعد الافتراق بطل سواء تجوز بها أو لا، وإن استبدل في المجلس صح وتمام التفريعات في البدائع وفي الصغرى : المسلم إليه إذا أتى بشيء من الدراهم وقال وجدته زيوفاً فالقول له .اهـ. وفي الإيضاح : استحسن أبو حنيفة في اليسير فقال يردها ويستبدل في ذلك المجلس وفي تحديد الكثير روايتان ما زاد على الثلث وما زاد على النصف اهـ وفيه لو وجد البعض نبهرجة أو مستحقة فاختلفا فقال رب السلم هو ثلث رأس المال وقال المسلم إليه نصفه فالقول قول رب السلم مع يمينه، ولو كان ستوقة أو رصاصاً فاختلفا في مثل ذلك فالقول قول المسلم إليه وبيانه فيه اهـ.
And if he finds it to be counterfeit (zuyuf), or mixed with base metal (nabahrajah), or stolen (stuqqah), or lead (rasas). If they are counterfeit and he accepts them, it is permissible absolutely, because they are not of the same type as his right. If he does not accept, then if it is before separation and he replaces them in the session, it is valid; if after separation, it is invalid according to Imam Abu Hanifa absolutely, whether he replaces them in the session or not. This is if he finds them counterfeit or mixed. If he finds them stolen or lead, then after separation it is invalid whether he accepts them or not, but if he replaces them in the session, it is valid. The complete subdivisions are in al-Bada'i. In al-Sughra: The seller (muslam ilayh) if he brings some dirhams and says 'I found them counterfeit', then his word is taken. In al-Idah: Abu Hanifa considered istihsan in small quantity: he returns them and replaces in that session. Regarding the definition of 'much', there are two narrations: more than one-third or more than one-half. Also in it: If he finds part to be mixed or defective and they differ, the buyer says it is one-third of the capital, the seller says one-half, then the word is that of the buyer with his oath. If it is stolen or lead, they differ similarly, then the word is that of the seller, and its explanation is there.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage from the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq provides rulings on various defects in the capital of a salam contract. The passage type is a ruling statement (memorization anchor). The ruling covers several scenarios: 1) If the capital is found to be counterfeit (zuyuf) or mixed with base metal (nabahrajah): if the buyer accepts, it is permissible; if not, and they are still in the session of contract, he can replace and it is valid; after separation, the contract becomes void according to Imam Abu Hanifa. 2) If the capital is stolen (stuqqah) or lead (rasas): after separation, the contract is void regardless of acceptance; but if replaced in the session, it is valid. The text also references detailed subdivisions in al-Bada'i. Additional rulings: The seller's statement regarding finding the capital counterfeit is accepted. Imam Abu Hanifa applied istihsan for a small quantity (yaseer) allowing return and replacement in the same session. There are two narrations for defining 'much' (kathir): more than one-third or more than one-half. If only part of the capital is defective and the parties disagree on the proportion, the buyer's word is taken with his oath for mixed/defective, but for stolen/lead, the seller's word is taken.

Key Terms
زيوف (zuyuf): Counterfeit coins that are not genuine currency.
نبهرجة (nabahrajah): Coins mixed with base metal, resembling genuine coins but of lower quality.
ستوقة (stuqqah): Stolen goods; here referring to stolen coins used as capital.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the buyer finds the capital to be counterfeit (zuyuf) after separation and does not accept, what is the ruling according to Imam Abu Hanifa?
A) The contract is valid if he replaces them in the session.
B) The contract is valid only if he accepts them.
C) The contract becomes invalid absolutely, even if he replaces them.
D) The contract is valid regardless.

Key Takeaway
Counterfeit or mixed capital in salam: acceptance validates, replacement before separation validates, but after separation the contract becomes void according to Imam Abu Hanifa.

Rulings on Stolen and Lead Capital in Salam and Subsidiary Rules
Rulings on stolen/lead capital and subsidiary rules

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when salam capital is stolen or made of lead, apply the istihsan rule for small defects, and resolve disputes over the proportion of defective capital.

Core Idea
Stolen or lead capital voids the contract after separation; small defects allow replacement via istihsan; disputes over proportion are resolved by oath of the buyer or seller depending on defect type.

Salam capital may be defective in more severe ways. This lesson covers stolen (stuqqah) or lead (rasas) capital, which are treated strictly regardless of acceptance. Additionally, it presents Imam Abu Hanifa's istihsan for small quantities, the definition of 'much', and rules for resolving disagreements between buyer and seller about the extent of the defect.

Guided Path
1
Identify stolen/lead capital ruling
If the capital is stolen or lead, after separation the contract becomes invalid whether the buyer accepts it or not. Replacement before separation in the session can save the contract.
This is stricter than for counterfeit capital.
2
Understand istihsan for small defect
Imam Abu Hanifa applied istihsan: if the defective quantity is small (yaseer), the buyer can return it and replace with genuine capital in the same session.
The contract is preserved through this concession.
3
Define 'much' (kathir)
There are two narrations: more than one-third of the capital or more than one-half. This threshold determines whether the defect is considered small or large.
The two views are both found in Hanafi sources.
4
Resolve dispute on proportion
If the parties disagree on the proportion of defective capital: for zuyuf/nabahrajah, the buyer's word with his oath is taken; for stuqqah/rasas, the seller's word with his oath is taken.
This reflects the presumption of ownership and control.
5
Apply the rules in practice
Combine these rules to handle cases where capital has partial defects or when the parties conflict over the extent.
The complete explanation is in al-Bada'i.

Set-off (Muqassa) in Salam Contract
Detailed rules of set-off in salam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for valid set-off in a salam contract, distinguish between debts arising from contract vs possession, and apply the rules of quality disparity and consent.

Core Idea
Set-off in salam is valid only if the debt arises by possession or by prior contract with mutual consent.

In salam contracts, parties may have mutual debts. The Hanafi school provides detailed rules on when these debts can be set off against the capital or subject matter. This lesson unpacks the conditions based on the source of the debt (contract vs possession), its timing, and the quality and consent of the parties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the source of the other debt
Determine whether the debt owed by one party to the other arises by contract (aqd) or by possession (qabd) such as usurpation (ghasb) or loan (qard).
This classification determines the subsequent rules.
2
Consider timing relative to the salam contract
If the debt arose by contract, check whether that contract was prior to or after the salam. If by possession, note whether it occurred before or after the salam.
Timing affects the need for consent and the possibility of set-off.
3
Assess equality of the debts
If the two debts are equal in quantity and quality, further rules apply. If they differ in quality (superior vs inferior), the consent of the owner of the superior quality is required.
Quality disparity can block set-off even if quantities match.
4
Apply the consent rule
For debts arising from a prior contract, set-off requires mutual consent. For debts arising from possession after the contract, set-off is automatic. For quality disparity, the superior owner's consent is mandatory.
Consent may be explicit or implied by actions.
5
Extend to set-off with the subject matter (muslam fih)
The same principles apply when the debt is similar to the salam subject matter, with additional nuances for trusts (wadi'ah) and usurped goods.
Trusts require physical handover; usurped goods must match in quality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسلم إليه دين مثل رأس المال فلا يخلوا إما أن يجب الدين الآخر بالعقد أو بالقبض، فإن كان الأول فإما بعقد سابق على السلم أو متأخر عنه، فإن كان الأول بأن كان رب السلم باع المسلم إليه ثوباً بعشرة دراهم ولم يقبضها حتى أسلم إليه عشرة دراهم في كر فإن تراضياً بالمقاصة صار قصاصاً، وإن أبى أحدهما لا يصير قصاصاً استحساناً لأن العقد موجب للقبض حقيقة لولا المقاصة، فإذا تقاصا تبين أنه انعقد موجباً قبضاً بطريق المقاصة وقد وجد، وإن وجب بعقد متأخر عن السلم لا يصير قصاصاً وإن جعلاه قصاصاً. هذا إذا وجب الدين بالعقد، فإن وجب بالقبض كالغصب والقرض فإنه يصير قصاصاً جعلاه أو لا بعد أن كان وجوب الدين متأخراً عن العقد هذا إذا تساوى الدينان، فإما إذا تفاضلا بأن كان أحدهما أفضل والآخر أدون ورضي أحدهما بالقصاص وأبى الآخر فإنه ينظر؛ فإن أبى صاحب الأفضل لا . قصاصاً لأن حقه يصير في الجودة معصوم محترم فلا يجوز إبطاله عليه من غير رضاه، وإن أبى صاحب الأدون يصير قصاصاً لأنه لما رضي به صاحب الأفضل فقد أسقط حقه، وكذلك المقاصة في بدلة الصرف على هذه التفاصيل؛ كذا في البدائع. قال الأزهري رحمه الله تعالى : الكر ستون قفيزاً، والقفيز ثمانية مكاكيك، والمكوك صاع ونصف. وفي الحسامي : الكراسم لأربعين قفيزاً. وهذا كله في رأس المال، أما المقاصة بالمسلم فيه فقال في الإيضاح : إن وجب على رب السلم دين مثل المسلم فيه بسبب متقدم على العقد أو بعده لم قصاصاً، وإن وجب بقبض مضمون كالغصب والقرض صار قصاصاً إن كان قبل يصر العقد، وإن كان بعده فجعله قصاصاً جاز. وإن كان وديعة عند رب السلم قبل العقد أو بعده فجعله المسلم إليه قصاصاً لم يكن قصاصاً إلا أن يكون بحضرتهما أو يخلى بينه وبينهما، ولا يصير المغصوب قصاصاً إلا إذا كان مثل المسلم فيه فإن كان أجود أو أردأ فلا بد من رضاهما اهـ.
The seller (muslam ilayh) has a debt similar to the capital. It is either that the other debt is obligatory by contract or by possession. If the first, then either by a contract prior to the salam or after it. If prior, such as the buyer sold the seller a garment for ten dirhams and did not receive it until he paid him ten dirhams in salam for a load, then if they both agree to set-off, it becomes set-off; if one refuses, it does not become set-off by istihsan, because the contract requires actual possession were it not for the set-off. So if they set-off, it becomes clear that the contract was concluded requiring possession by way of set-off, and it has occurred. If the debt arises from a contract after the salam, it does not become set-off even if they make it a set-off. This is if the debt arises by contract. If it arises by possession (qabd) such as usurpation (ghasb) or loan (qard), then it becomes set-off whether they make it so or not, as long as the obligation of the debt is after the contract. This is if the two debts are equal. If they differ, one being superior (better quality) and the other inferior, and one consents to set-off and the other refuses, then if the owner of the superior refuses, it is not set-off because his right to quality is protected and inviolable; it cannot be voided without his consent. If the owner of the inferior refuses, it becomes set-off because the owner of the superior has waived his right. Similarly for set-off in the exchange of currency (badal al-sarf) with these details. Al-Azhari (may God have mercy on him) said: 'A kurr is sixty qafiz, a qafiz is eight makakik, a makkuk is one and a half sa'. According to al-Husami: 'A kurr is forty qafiz.' All this refers to the capital. As for set-off with the subject matter (muslam fih), al-Idah says: If a debt similar to the subject matter becomes obligatory on the buyer due to a contract before or after, it is not set-off. If it arises by possessing something guaranteed (ghasb or qard), it becomes set-off if it occurs before the contract; if after, then if he makes it set-off, it is permissible. If it is a trust (wadi'ah) with the buyer before the contract or after, and the seller makes it set-off, it is not set-off unless it is in their presence or he vacates possession to him. Usurped property does not become set-off unless it is like the subject matter; if it is of better or worse quality, mutual consent is required.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses the rules of set-off (muqassa) in salam contracts, focusing on when a debt owed by one party can be set off against the capital or subject matter. The passage type is a ruling with proof, using istihsan and qiyas. The commentary first considers the case where the seller owes a debt similar to the capital. The debt arises either by contract (aqd) or by possession (qabd). If by contract, it matters whether the contract is prior to or after the salam. If prior, set-off is valid if both agree; otherwise not by istihsan, because the contract implied actual possession, but set-off can substitute. If after, set-off is invalid even with consent. If the debt arises by possession (like ghasb or qard), set-off is automatic regardless of consent, provided the debt is after the contract. If the debts are unequal in quality, the owner of the superior quality must consent for set-off to be valid; if the inferior owner refuses, set-off is forced. The same rules apply to set-off in currency exchange (sarf). The passage then defines a kurr: according to al-Azhari, 60 qafiz; each qafiz 8 makakik; each makkuk 1.5 sa'. Another opinion says 40 qafiz. The discussion then turns to set-off with the subject matter (muslam fih): If a debt similar to the subject matter arises by contract, it cannot be set off. If by qabd (ghasb/qard), set-off is valid if it occurs before the salam contract; if after, it is permissible with mutual agreement. Trust deposits (wadi'ah) require the item to be in the parties' presence or vacated. Usurped goods require the item to be of the same type; if different quality, consent is needed.

Key Terms
المقاصة (al-muqassa): Set-off; mutual cancellation of reciprocal debts.
القصاص (al-qisas): Here used interchangeably with muqassa (set-off).
الكر (al-kurr): A measure of volume, varying according to different authorities: 60 qafiz or 40 qafiz.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a salam contract, a debt arises from a contract after the salam. Both parties agree to set it off against the capital. According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling?
A) Valid with mutual consent.
B) Invalid even with mutual consent.
C) Valid only if the debt is of the same quality.
D) Valid only if the debt is by possession.

Key Takeaway
Set-off in salam is conditionally valid; the source and timing of the debt, quality parity, and consent determine the ruling.

Disposal of Salam Capital and Subject Matter Before Possession
Ruling on disposal of salam capital and subject matter before possession

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on disposal of salam capital and subject matter before possession and apply it to shirkah and tawliyah scenarios.

Core Idea
Disposing of salam capital or subject matter before possession is invalid because possession is incomplete.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a key rule in salam contracts: neither the capital (ra's al-mal) nor the subject matter (muslam fih) can be disposed of before possession is taken. The text explains the legal reasoning and gives two concrete forms of prohibited disposal: shirkah (partnership) and tawliyah (assignment at cost). Understanding this ruling helps prevent common mistakes in salam transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
The text states: "ولايصح التصرف في رأس المال والمسلم فيه قبل القبض" meaning it is not valid to dispose of the capital or subject matter before possession. Recognize that this is a prohibition (tahrim) rendering the disposal invalid.
The term "لا يصح" indicates the act is void and impermissible.
2
Understand the Rationale
The subject matter is a sold good (mabee') and disposing of it before possession is prohibited by hadith. The capital is due to be received in the session, and disposal would forfeit that obligation.
The hadith forbidding sale before receipt (e.g., 'لا تبع ما ليس عندك') is the basis.
3
Apply to Shirkah and Tawliyah
In shirkah, the buyer says: 'Give me half the capital so you get half the subject matter.' This is a transfer of part ownership for compensation before possession, thus invalid. In tawliyah, the buyer says: 'Give me the like of what I gave' — effectively selling the capital before possession.
Both are forms of disposal for compensation; gratuitous disposal (hiba) might have a different ruling.
4
Note the Talil (Legal Cause)
The underlying cause is that possession is a condition for valid disposal of both capital and subject matter. The capital is a debt (dayn) due in the session, and the subject matter is a specific good not yet received.
This is consistent with the general principle that one cannot dispose of something not in one's possession.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولا يصح التصرف في رأس المال والمسلم فيه قبل القبض بشركة أو تولية) لأن المسلم فيه مبيع والتصرف في المبيع المنقول قبل القبض لا يجوز ورأس المال مستحق القبض في المجلس والتصرف فيه مفوت له فلم يجز ففي التولية تمليكه بعوض، وفي الشركة تمليك بعضه بعوض فلم يجز وصورة الشركة فيه أن يقول رب السلم لآخر أعطني نصف رأس المال ليكون نصف المسلم لك فيه وصورة التولية أن يقول لآخر اعطني مثل ما أعطيت . الرملي انظره مع ما يأتي قريباً من قوله وقيد بكونه جعل الدين عليه رأس مال لأن لو لم يجعله وإنما وقعت المقاصة الخ والظاهر أنه أي الذي يأتي مقابل الصحيح وهو من كلام البدائع تأمل اهـ . قلت : وفي المسألة الآتية تفاصيل يمكن حمل ما هنا على بعض منها تأمل قوله : (الكر ستون قفيزا الخ) فيكون القفيز اثني عشر صاعاً ويكون الكر سبعمائة وعشرين صاعاً وذلك أربع غراير ونصف شامية
His statement (may God have mercy on him): (And it is not valid to dispose of the capital and the subject matter (muslam fih) before possession by way of partnership (shirkah) or assignment (tawliyah)) because the subject matter is a sold good, and disposal of a movable sold good before possession is not permissible. The capital is due for receipt in the session, and disposal of it would forfeit that, so it is not allowed. In tawliyah, it is a transfer of ownership for compensation; in shirkah, it is a transfer of part ownership for compensation, so it is not permitted. The form of shirkah is that the buyer (rabb al-salam) says to another: 'Give me half the capital so that half of the subject matter is for you.' The form of tawliyah is that he says to another: 'Give me the like of what I gave.' Al-Ramli: 'Look at what is coming soon from his statement and the restriction of making the debt the capital, because if he had not made it, and rather set-off occurred etc., and it appears that what is coming is in contrast to the sound view, and it is from the words of al-Bada'i; reflect.' I say: In the upcoming issue there are details that can support some of what is here; reflect on his statement: (A kurr is sixty qafiz etc.) So a qafiz becomes twelve sa', and a kurr becomes seven hundred and twenty sa', which is four and a half Syrian ghara'ir.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the ruling that it is not valid to dispose of the capital of a salam contract or the subject matter (muslam fih) before taking possession. The passage type is a ruling statement with explanation. The rationale: The subject matter is a sold good (mabee'), and disposing of a movable sold good before possession is prohibited. The capital is due to be received in the session; any disposal would prevent that. The specific types of disposal discussed are shirkah (partnership) and tawliyah (assignment at cost). In shirkah, the buyer offers to share the capital with a third party in exchange for a share of the subject matter; this is invalid because it is a sale of part before possession. In tawliyah, the buyer asks another to give him the same amount as he gave, effectively selling the capital before possession. The passage also includes a note from al-Ramli warning the reader to compare with what follows (the earlier discussion on set-off) and an observation that the upcoming issue (in the text) has details that may apply here. Finally, there is a definition of a kurr as 60 qafiz, which with a qafiz of 12 sa' gives 720 sa', equivalent to 4.5 Syrian ghara'ir.

Key Terms
التولية (al-tawliyah): A sale or transfer of ownership at the original cost price, without profit.
الشركة (al-shirkah): Partnership; here meaning inviting a third party to share the capital in exchange for a share of the subject matter.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is disposal of the capital before possession invalid in salam?
A) Because the capital is not yet owned by the seller.
B) Because the capital is due to be received in the session and disposal would prevent that.
C) Because the subject matter is not yet delivered.
D) Because it leads to usury.

Key Takeaway
In salam, neither the capital nor the subject matter can be disposed of before possession; any compensated transfer (shirkah or tawliyah) before possession is invalid.

Set-off of the Capital in Salam (Muqassa)
Explains the conditions for set-off between the salam capital and a debt from another transaction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for set-off (muqassa) in a salam contract and apply the ruling to scenarios involving debts by contract or receipt, including unequal debts.

Core Idea
Set-off in salam depends on the nature of the debt: by contract requires consent, by receipt is automatic.

In a salam contract, the capital may be set off against a separate debt owed by the seller to the buyer. However, the Hanafi school lays out specific conditions depending on how that debt arose and its timing. This lesson will guide you through the key scenarios, distinguishing between debts incurred by contract and those by receipt, and the rules when debts are unequal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of debt
Determine whether the separate debt is incurred by contract (e.g., a sale) or by receipt (e.g., ghasb or qard). This determines the baseline rule.
Debts by receipt are treated more strictly for set-off.
2
Determine the timing relative to salam
If the debt is by contract, check if it was prior to the salam contract or subsequent. This affects the validity of set-off.
Prior debts can be set off with mutual consent; subsequent debts cannot be set off at all.
3
Apply the consent rule
For a debt by contract prior to salam, set-off requires mutual consent (istihsan). If one party refuses, it does not take effect. For a debt by receipt, set-off is automatic regardless of consent.
Consent is not needed for receipt-based debts.
4
Handle unequal debts
If the two debts are of different quality, the owner of the superior quality cannot be forced to set-off; his right is inviolable. The owner of the inferior quality can be compelled if the other party agrees.
This protects the holder of the superior debt.
5
Note the application to currency exchange
The same details apply to set-off in currency exchange (badalah al-sarf), indicating a broader principle.
Currency exchange (sarf) involves immediate exchange of counter-values.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسلم إليه دين مثل رأس المال فلا يخلوا إما أن يجب الدين الآخر بالعقد أو بالقبض، فإن كان الأول فإما بعقد سابق على السلم أو متأخر عنه، فإن كان الأول بأن كان رب السلم باع المسلم إليه ثوباً بعشرة دراهم ولم يقبضها حتى أسلم إليه عشرة دراهم في كر فإن تراضياً بالمقاصة صار قصاصاً، وإن أبى أحدهما لا يصير قصاصاً استحساناً لأن العقد موجب للقبض حقيقة لولا المقاصة، فإذا تقاصا تبين أنه انعقد موجباً قبضاً بطريق المقاصة وقد وجد، وإن وجب بعقد متأخر عن السلم لا يصير قصاصاً وإن جعلاه قصاصاً. هذا إذا وجب الدين بالعقد، فإن وجب بالقبض كالغصب والقرض فإنه يصير قصاصاً جعلاه أو لا بعد أن كان وجوب الدين متأخراً عن العقد هذا إذا تساوى الدينان، فإما إذا تفاضلا بأن كان أحدهما أفضل والآخر أدون ورضي أحدهما بالقصاص وأبى الآخر فإنه ينظر؛ فإن أبى صاحب الأفضل لا . قصاصاً لأن حقه يصير في الجودة معصوم محترم فلا يجوز إبطاله عليه من غير رضاه، وإن أبى صاحب الأدون يصير قصاصاً لأنه لما رضي به صاحب الأفضل فقد أسقط حقه، وكذلك المقاصة في بدلة الصرف على هذه التفاصيل؛ كذا في البدائع. قال الأزهري رحمه الله تعالى : الكر ستون قفيزاً، والقفيز ثمانية مكاكيك، والمكوك صاع ونصف. وفي الحسامي : الكراسم لأربعين قفيزاً. وهذا كله في رأس المال
The one receiving salam (muslam ilayh) owes a debt similar to the capital. So either that other debt is incurred by contract or by receipt. If by contract, it is either prior to the salam contract or subsequent. If prior, e.g., the salam seller sold the buyer a cloth for ten dirhams and had not received them until he advanced ten dirhams for a karr (of wheat), then if they mutually agree to set-off, it becomes a set-off; but if one refuses, it does not become a set-off by istihsan because the contract necessitates receipt actually were it not for the set-off; when they do set-off, it becomes clear that the contract was concluded as a receipt via set-off and it has occurred. If the debt is incurred by a contract subsequent to salam, it does not become a set-off even if they make it a set-off. This is when the debt is incurred by contract. If it is incurred by receipt, like usurpation (ghasb) or loan (qard), then it becomes a set-off whether they make it so or not, provided that the obligation of the debt is after the contract. This is when the two debts are equal. If they are unequal, e.g., one is superior and the other inferior, and one agrees to set-off but the other refuses, then we consider: if the owner of the superior refuses, it does not become a set-off because his right in the quality is protected and inviolable; it is not permissible to nullify it without his consent. If the owner of the inferior refuses, it becomes a set-off because the owner of the superior has agreed and thus dropped his right. The same applies to set-off in the price of currency exchange (badalah al-sarf) with these details; as in al-Bada'i'. Al-Azhari said: the karr is sixty qafiz, the qafiz is eight makakik, and the makkuk is one and a half sa'. In al-Husami: the karr is a term for forty qafiz. All of this pertains to the capital.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement explaining the conditions under which set-off (muqassa) occurs between the capital of a salam contract and a separate debt owed to the salam seller by the buyer. The ruling distinguishes between debts incurred by contract and debts incurred by receipt (like qard or ghasb). Key scenarios: if the debt is prior to salam, set-off requires mutual consent; if subsequent, set-off is invalid. For debts by receipt, set-off is automatic. When debts are unequal, the owner of the superior quality cannot be forced to set-off, while the owner of the inferior can be compelled. The passage also includes a definition of the 'karr' unit of measurement commonly used in salam.

Key Terms
المقاصة (al-muqassa): The mutual cancellation of two debts; set-off.
الكر (al-karr): A unit of dry measure, defined as 60 qafiz (or 40 in another opinion).
القبض (al-qabd): Receipt; taking possession.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a salam contract, the buyer owes the seller a debt from a prior sale (contract). The seller refuses to set off. Does the set-off become effective?
A) Yes, because the debts are similar.
B) No, because set-off requires mutual consent for contractual debts.
C) Yes, because the debt predates the salam contract.
D) No, because the debt is by receipt.

Key Takeaway
Set-off (muqassa) in salam is conditional: contractual debts require consent, receipt debts are automatic, and unequal debts protect the superior quality owner.
